Refuting the Mu'tazilite Analogy of Creation
Refutes the Mu'tazilite analogy between divine and human creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi refutation of the Mu'tazilite analogy between divine and human creation, focusing on the principle that similarity is determined by essence, not by the agent.

Core Idea
Similarity of actions depends on essence, not on who performs them.

This passage presents a key argument from Imam al-Nasafi against the Mu'tazilite claim that human creation resembles God's creation. The author establishes a logical principle: two things similar in essence are not made different by external factors like the agent. He then applies this to the Mu'tazilite position and shows its theological consequences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Principle
Read the first key sentence: things similar in essence do not differ due to agent or external factors. Grasp that similarity is based on essence itself.
This is the foundational logical rule.
2
Examine the Examples
Look at the examples given: movements from different directions, movement vs rest, black vs white, etc. Notice how even with the same agent, these are not considered similar.
The examples show that essence matters, not agent or circumstance.
3
Apply to the Mu'tazilite Claim
Apply the principle: the Mu'tazila said human creation is similar to God's creation. According to the rule, if the essences are similar, varying agents does not affect similarity. But God negated that in the Quran.
God's negation proves the claim false.
4
Reflect on the Conclusion
The author concludes that affirming creative power for other than Allah leads to impossibility and disbelief. Understand why this is a serious charge.
The argument preserves divine uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: قد سبق الكلام أن المشتبهين بذاتيهما لا يختلفان باختلاف الفاعلين ولا باختلاف معاني وراء ذاتيهما، ولا عبرة لاختلاف الفاعل واتحاده، إنما العبرة في معرفة حقيقة الاشتباه لذات المشتبهين أو المعنى الموجب للاشتباه لا لما وراء ذلك من الفاعل أو المكان أو الزمان أو الآلة أو السبب، لأن كل ذلك لا يوجب التغير في علة الاشتباه أو الاختلاف، ولهذا لم يكن بين الحركتين من جهتين مختلفتين وبين الحركة والسكون وبين السواد والبياض والاجتماع والافتراق والحلاوة والمرارة والنتن والرائحة الطيبة مشابهة عند اتحاد الفاعل، فكذا لا يوجب اختلاف الفاعلين اختلافا في الفعلين عند اشتباههما بذاتيهما. وتحقق ما رمنا تحقيقه من إثبات المشابهة بين خلق الله تعالى وخلق العباد على أصول المعتزلة، والله تعالى نفى ذلك فصاروا بذلك رادين على الله تعالى وجاعلين أنفسهم مستحقة للعبادة وأهلاً لها لأن الله تعالى بقوله: ﴿ أَمْ جَعَلُوا لِلَّهِ شُرَكَاءَ خَلَقُوا كَخَلْقِهِ فَتَشَابَهَ الْخَلْقُ عَلَيْهِمْ ﴾ أوجب لهم العذر في عبادتهم ما كانوا يعبدون وجعلهم إياهم شركاء الله تعالى في العبادة لو كان منهم خلق كخلقه، وكل ذلك كفر عصمنا الله تعالى عن مثل ذلك. وبالوقوف على ما بينا ظهرت صحة ما ادعينا أن إثبات قدرة التخليق لغير الله تعالى يؤدي إلى المحال، وبالله التوفيق.
Then we say: It has already been stated that two things that are similar in their essences do not differ due to the difference of agents nor due to differences of meanings beyond their essences, and no consideration is given to the difference or unity of the agent; consideration is only given to knowing the reality of the similarity in the essence of the two similar things or the meaning that necessitates similarity, not to what is beyond that of the agent, place, time, instrument, or cause, because all of that does not necessitate change in the cause of similarity or difference. For this reason, there is no similarity between two movements from two different directions, nor between movement and rest, nor between blackness and whiteness, nor between conjunction and separation, nor between sweetness and bitterness, nor between stench and pleasant fragrance when the agent is one. Likewise, the difference of agents does not necessitate a difference in the two actions when they are similar in their essences. And what we aimed to establish is realized: the proof of similarity between the creation of Allah Almighty and the creation of servants according to the principles of the Mu'tazila, but Allah Almighty negated that, so they thereby became rejecters of Allah and made themselves worthy of worship and deserving of it because Allah Almighty by His saying: 'Or have they attributed to Allah partners who created like His creation, so the creation seemed similar to them?' made for them an excuse in worshipping what they used to worship and made them partners of Allah in worship if there were from them a creation like His creation. And all of that is disbelief; may Allah protect us from such. And by stopping at what we clarified, the correctness of what we claimed becomes apparent: that affirming the power of originating creation for other than Allah Almighty leads to impossibility, and success is with Allah.
Source pages p. 899

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite analogy between human creation and divine creation. The author begins by restating a fundamental principle: two things that are similar in their essences do not differ due to external factors such as the agent, place, time, etc. The only thing that matters is the essence itself or the meaning that causes the similarity. He gives examples: movements from different directions, movement and rest, black and white, etc., are not considered similar even if the agent is the same. Therefore, the fact that the agents differ (God and humans) does not make the actions similar if they are indeed similar in essence. The Mu'tazila claimed that if humans could create like God, then the creation would be similar, but God negated that in the Quran (13:16). The author points out that the Mu'tazila by affirming this similarity are actually rejecting God's word and making themselves deserving of worship, because if human creation were like God's, then worshipping idols would be excused. He concludes that affirming creative power for other than God leads to impossibility, which is the main point.

Key Terms
قدرة التخليق
المشتبهين بذاتيهما
الاختلاف
الاشتباه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites believed that because humans can produce actions (like writing or building), those actions are similar in essence to God's creation, and thus the agent's identity (God vs. human) is irrelevant for determining similarity.
Correction: The author argues that similarity is determined by the essence of the actions themselves, not by the agent. In reality, human actions and divine creation are fundamentally different in essence; the fact that the agents differ does not make them similar. Moreover, the Quran expressly negates any similarity between God's creation and that of others.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the only relevant factor for determining similarity between two things?
A) The identity of the agent
B) The essence or quality that necessitates similarity
C) The time and place of the actions
D) The instrument used

Key Takeaway
Affirming creative power for other than Allah leads to logical impossibility and contradicts the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself acting as if your abilities and achievements are entirely your own, as if you create outcomes independently of Allah? In that moment, are you not subtly echoing the Mu'tazilite claim that human action is like divine creation? What would it take to truly dissolve that illusion in your heart today?
?
The Mu'tazila were accused of making themselves worthy of worship by asserting creative power. In what ways might you be exalting your own intellect, will, or effort to a level that rivals Allah's sovereignty? How can you repent from that and reassert His sole lordship over your life?
?
If you truly believed that no one creates like Allah, would you still worry about your future as you do? Would you still cling to your plans and strategies as if they determine your destiny? What would change in your daily attitude if this lesson penetrated your soul?

The Servant's Action Without Creative Power
States that the servant has action based on proofs, though lacking creative power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Maturidi school affirms real human actions while denying humans the power to create or originate those acts, based on scriptural, rational, and necessary proofs.

Core Idea
Humans act, but only Allah creates the act.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi explains a key Maturidi doctrine: the servant has genuine action, but not the ability to bring that action into existence from nothing. The evidence is drawn from scripture, reason, and undeniable necessary knowledge—proofs that even opponents accept. This teaching also serves as a refutation of the Jabriyya who deny human agency altogether.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the central claim: the servant has action but not creative power.
Notice the phrase 'قدرة التخليق' — the power to originate creation.
2
Identify the three proofs
The passage mentions three types of proofs: scriptural (السمعية), rational (العقلية), and necessary (الضرورة). Understand what each refers to.
The third type, necessary knowledge, is so immediate that denying it is stubbornness.
3
Connect to the broader context
Realize that this passage builds on earlier refutations of the Jabriyya, who claimed humans have no real action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العبد له فعل وليست له قدرة التخليق: ثم الدليل على أن العبد له فعل وإن لم تكن له قدرة التخليق مساعدة الخصوم إيانا أن له فعلاً، وما مر من الدلائل الموجبة لذلك وهي الدلائل السمعية والعقلية والضرورة التي يصير دافعها مكابرًا على ما سبق ذكره في إبطال كلام الجبرية.
The servant has action and does not have the power of originating creation: Then the proof that the servant has action, even though he does not have the power of originating creation, is the agreement of the opponents with us that he has action, and what has preceded of proofs necessitating that, which are the scriptural, rational, and necessary proofs, the rejecter of which becomes obstinate, as previously mentioned in refuting the speech of the Jabriyya.
Source pages p. 899

Commentary
This passage introduces the Maturidi position: the human servant does have actions, but he does not have the power to originate or create those actions from nothing (power of ikhtira' / تخليق). The author states that the evidence for this comes from three sources: scriptural proofs (السّمعيات), rational proofs (العقليات), and necessary knowledge (الضرورة) that even a stubborn person cannot deny. He refers back to an earlier refutation of the Jabriyya, who denied human agency altogether.

Key Terms
قدرة التخليق
فعل العبد
الدلائل السمعية
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if humans do not create their actions, they have no action at all.
Correction: The passage asserts that humans do have actions (فعل), even though they do not have creative power (قدرة التخليق). The action is real and attributable to the servant, while the creation of the action is from Allah alone.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position affirms real human action while denying humans the power to create those actions, supported by scriptural, rational, and necessary proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says denying human action is obstinacy. Have you ever fallen into either extreme—feeling completely helpless (Jabri) or feeling self-sufficient (Mu'tazili)? Reflect on a moment when you blamed your environment instead of your own choice. Will you now repent and accept your role?
?
If Allah alone creates the act, yet you are commanded to do it, then your striving is not about controlling outcomes but about obedience. In what area of your life do you most need to trust Allah's creation while exerting your own action? Commit to one specific act of worship today that you will do with full intention, leaving the result to Allah.

Objection One: Action Necessitates Invention
Objection and response regarding action and invention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that human action implies human invention, establishing that only God has the power of creation.

Core Idea
Having an action does not mean you invented it—invention is solely for Allah.

In this brief exchange, the opponent makes a seemingly logical leap: if we truly perform actions, then we must be the ones who bring them into existence. The author counters with a decisive theological distinction, reminding us that the power to invent belongs exclusively to God. Let's examine this objection and its response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opponent's claim that if the servant has action, he must be its inventor. Notice the logical assumption being made.
The opponent assumes a direct causal link between action and invention.
2
Identify the author's response
The author points to an already-established proof that only Allah has the power of invention. This breaks the assumed link.
The response does not deny that humans act; it denies the implication of invention.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why having an action does not require inventing it. In Maturidi theology, actions are created by God and acquired by humans.
This distinction is crucial for understanding human responsibility without associating partners with God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إذا ثبت أن العبد له فعل ثبت أنه هو المخترع له. قلنا: قد أقمنا الدلالة على أن ليس لغير الله تعالى قدرة الاختراع على وجه لم يبق للشك فيه مجال.
If they say: If it is established that the servant has action, then it is established that he is the inventor of it. We say: We have established the proof that other than Allah Almighty does not have the power of invention in a manner that leaves no room for doubt.
Source pages p. 900

Commentary
This is the first objection: the opponent argues that if the servant truly has an action, then he must be the one who invents or creates that action (المخترع). The author's response is brief: we have already proven conclusively that no one other than God has the power of invention (اختراع). Therefore, having action does not imply having inventive power. The two are distinct.

Key Terms
قدرة الاختراع
المخترع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that 'having an action' automatically means 'creating it ex nihilo'.
Correction: In Maturidi theology, humans acquire actions, but only God creates them from nothing. Action does not imply invention.

Checkpoint

D) He says the objection is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Action and invention are distinct; only Allah invents, while humans merely acquire actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

No Creative Power Implies No Action? The Answered Objection
Objection and response on human action without creative power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi response to the objection that lack of creative power eliminates human action, and how the intuitive distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions proves real human agency.

Core Idea
We know our actions exist by necessity, even without creating them.

This short passage captures a critical objection in the debate on human agency: if humans do not create their own actions, then they have no real action at all. The author responds by appealing to a basic human intuition—the clear distinction between forced actions and voluntary actions. This distinction, known by immediate experience, shows that the servant indeed has action, even if Allah is the Creator of all existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: if the servant lacks creative power, he cannot have any action and would be compelled.
This is a common criticism of the Maturidi position.
2
Identify the response
The author appeals to the necessary knowledge of the difference between necessary (involuntary) and voluntary actions.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Why doesn't the absence of creative power imply coercion? Because we directly experience choice in voluntary actions.
Consider examples like blinking vs. deliberate eye movement.
4
Connect to kasb
This argument relies on the concept of acquisition (kasb): human actions are real but not created by humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لو لم يكن للعبد قدرة التخليق لم يكن له فعل، وكان مضطرًا فيما يحصل منه من الأفعال. قلنا: قد أقمنا الدلالة على أن له فعلاً يعرف بطريق الضرورة الفرق بين الأفعال الضرورية وبين الأفعال الاختيارية.
If they say: If the servant does not have the power of originating creation, then he would have no action, and he would be compelled in what occurs from him of actions. We say: We have established the proof that he has action, known by way of necessity the distinction between necessary actions and voluntary actions.
Source pages p. 900

Commentary
The second objection argues that if the servant lacks the power to originate actions (قدرة التخليق), then he cannot have any action at all and would be coerced (مضطر) like inanimate objects. The author responds by reiterating that we have proven that the servant does have action, and this is known through necessary knowledge (الضرورة). People intuitively distinguish between voluntary actions (الأفعال الاختيارية) and involuntary or compelled actions (الأفعال الضرورية). For example, blinking vs. deliberate eye movement. This distinction exists even if the servant does not create the action ex nihilo.

Key Terms
الأفعال الضرورية
الأفعال الاختيارية
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If humans do not create their actions, then they cannot have any genuine action at all; they are mere puppets.
Correction: Humans do have genuine action, as proven by the necessary distinction between involuntary and voluntary actions. This distinction shows that human choices are real, even though Allah is the ultimate Creator of all existence.

Checkpoint
What distinction does the author use to prove the existence of the servant's action?
A) Between good and evil actions
B) Between necessary (involuntary) and voluntary actions
C) Between physical and mental actions
D) Between actions of obedience and disobedience

Key Takeaway
Even without creative power, human actions are real because we directly experience the difference between what we are forced to do and what we choose to do.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment today when you performed a voluntary action — maybe speaking a word, making a decision, or moving intentionally. Right then, did you feel compelled, or did you experience yourself as the one who chose and acted? How does that immediate, undeniable feeling of agency confront the claim that you have no real action?
?
If your voluntary actions are real yet created by Allah, what does that mean for the excuses you make for your sins — 'I couldn't help it,' 'I was forced by circumstances'? Does the knowledge you have by necessity (the difference between what you chose and what happened to you) strip away those excuses?
?
The objectors in the lesson argued that without creative power, humans are just passive. But you know from your own experience that you are not a rock or a machine. Why are you so quick to treat yourself as a passive victim of your habits, desires, or environment when accountability calls? What would it cost you to fully own your actions as truly yours before Allah?

Rational Proof vs. Imagination: The Conceivability of Divine and Human Coaction
Nasafi answers the objection that divine-human coaction is inconceivable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Islamic theology, 'conceivability' is defined by rational proof, not by human imagination, and why this distinction is crucial for the Maturidi position on human agency.

Core Idea
What is rationally proven is conceivable, even if unimaginable.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi responds to an objection that it is 'inconceivable' for both God and the servant to be involved in the same act. The objectors claim their imagination cannot picture such a scenario. Nasafi challenges their definition of 'conceivable', arguing that it should be based on rational evidence, not sensory imagination. He then provides two rational proofs that establish the possibility of divine-human coaction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: objection, then response.
The passage ends abruptly, but the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the objection and Nasafi's response
Find the objectors' claim: 'This is inconceivable (غير معقول).' Then see how Nasafi questions their definition and offers his own.
3
Understand Nasafi's definition of 'conceivable'
Nasafi defines 'conceivable' (المعقول) as 'that upon which rational proof has been established.' This is key to his argument.
This contrasts with the objectors' reliance on imagination.
4
Note the two rational proofs he cites
Listen for two proofs: (1) the impossibility of creative power for other than God, and (2) that the servant indeed has action. These establish the rational conceivability of coaction.
5
Reflect on the role of imagination
Nasafi explains that imagination (وهم) is based on sense perception (حس) and is limited. The fact that something is unimaginable does not mean it is impossible, especially when proof exists.
This refutes the common misunderstanding that 'unimaginable' equals 'impossible'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If they say: This is inconceivable that Allah Almighty brings the act from non-existence to existence and the servant is an agent. We say: Why do you say it is inconceivable? The conceivable is that upon which rational proof has been established, and proof has been established in our case. For the proof has been established of the impossibility of the existence of the power of originating creation for other than Allah Almighty, and proof has been established that the servant has action. However, this is not imagined in your minds because you have not seen in the witnessed realm someone who has the power of action in the locus of another's power and someone who has the power of invention to do his voluntary act and bring it into existence in the locus of his power. So it is not imagined in your minds due to the deficiency of your senses in stopping upon it. And imagination is a result of sense, as previously mentioned, and the exit of a thing from imagination does not necessitate the impossibility of its existence when proof has been established for its existence. Indeed, knowledge...

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that it is rationally inconceivable (غير معقول) for God to create the act while the servant is simultaneously the agent. The author argues that 'conceivable' is defined by rational proof, not by imagination. Since rational proofs have established both that God alone has creative power and that the servant does have actions, the combination is rationally established, even if it is not imaginable. The limitation lies in the human senses and imagination, which are based on sense perception (الحس). What is unimaginable is not necessarily impossible if proof supports it. The passage is cut off at the word 'العلم', but the argument is essentially complete: the absence of imagination does not negate rational proof.

One Act Under Two Powers
Author proves permissibility of one act under two powers via consensus and logical necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for how one act can be under both God's power and human power, based on consensus and the impossibility of God empowering over what He cannot do.

Core Idea
One act can be under two powers: God’s and the servant’s.

This passage tackles a key theological puzzle: if God is the sole creator, how can human actions also be attributed to humans? Imam al-Nasafi argues that it is not only possible but necessary that one act falls under two powers. He builds on earlier proofs and consensus to demonstrate that the Mu'tazilite rejection is based on imagination, not reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Let the argument flow without overanalyzing. Notice how the author references earlier proofs.
The passage contains footnote markers (۳), (٤), etc., which indicate scholarly references.
2
Identify the core premises
Find where the author says God empowers the servant (consensus) and that God cannot empower over what He has no power over.
These premises lead to the conclusion that the act must be under both powers.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila refutation
Notice that the author dismisses their objection as based on imagination (wahm), not reason.
This highlights the methodological difference between Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين جائز] وبالوقوف على (۳) ما بينا من دليل استحالة ثبوت قدرة التخليق(٤) للعبد، ودليل ثبوت الفعل والقدرة له يعرف جواز دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين (۹) بحمد الله تعالى دليل فيه كفاية وغنية عن الاشتغال بغيره من الدلائل. وذلك ثم نقول: قد سبق منا القول في الفصل الذي أثبتنا فيه استحالة دخول الاختراع تحت قدرة العبد، وأن إقدار الغير على ما لا يقدر عليه المقدر (۲) محال، وقد انعقد الإجماع أن الله تعالى هو الذي يقدر العبد ويخلق قدرته، وأن القدرة ليست بداخله تحت قدرة العبد إذ لو منعت منه لما تمكن من اكتسابها عندنا وتخليقها عندهم، وإذا كان الله تعالى هو الذي يقدر العبد كان محالاً أن يقدره على ما لاقدرة له عليه على ما قررنا، فكان ذلك دليلاً على جواز دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين، ثم لا دليل يدل (۲) للمعتزلة على استحالة دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين، إلا أنهم لم يعاينوا ذلك وهم أبدا يفزعون إلى الوهم ويجعلونه عيارا للعقل والفهم وهو بعيد عن الصواب على ما قررنا.
[The entry of one act of power under the power of two powers is permissible.] And by reflecting on what we have clarified of the proof of the impossibility of establishing the power of creation for the servant, and the proof of the establishment of action and power for him, one knows the permissibility of one act of power under the power of two powers – by the praise of God – a proof sufficient and dispensing with the need for other proofs. And that: Then we say: We have already stated in the chapter in which we established the impossibility of the entry of creation under the power of the servant, and that empowering another over what the one who empowers is not capable of is impossible. And the consensus has been concluded that God, exalted, is the One who empowers the servant and creates his power, and that the power [of the servant] is not within the power of the servant – because if it were prevented from him, he would not be able to acquire it according to us, nor to create it according to them. And when God, exalted, is the One who empowers the servant, it is impossible that He empower him over what He has no power over, as we have explained. So that became a proof for the permissibility of one act of power under the power of two powers. Then there is no proof indicating to the Mu'tazila the impossibility of one act of power under the power of two powers, except that they do not witness that, and they always flee to imagination (wahm) and make it the criterion for intellect and understanding, and that is far from correctness as we have clarified.
Source pages p. 905

Commentary
This passage provides a proof for the permissibility (jawāz) of one act (maqdūr) being under the power (qudra) of two powerful agents (qādirān). The author begins by stating that by considering two earlier proofs – the proof of the impossibility of the servant having creative power, and the proof of the servant's capacity for action and power – one can understand this permissibility. The proof is self-sufficient. He then elaborates: They had previously established that creation (ikhtirāʿ) cannot fall under the servant's power. Moreover, it is impossible for one being to empower another over something that the empowering being itself is not capable of. There is consensus (ijmāʿ) that God is the one who empowers the servant and creates his power. And that the servant's power is not itself within the servant's power (i.e., the servant does not create his own power). If the servant's power were prevented from him, he would not be able to acquire (kasb) it (according to the Ahl al-Sunna) nor create it (according to the Mu'tazila). Since God empowers the servant, He cannot empower him over something He himself has no power over. This implies that the act must be within God's power as well, thus one act is under two powers: God's and the servant's. The author then criticizes the Mu'tazila for lacking a proof against this, and for relying on mere imagination (wahm) instead of reason.

Key Terms
مقدور واحد
قدرة قادرين
إجماع
وهم
اختراع
اكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that two powers over one act implies partnership in divinity (shirk).
Correction: The author distinguishes between God's creative power (takhlīq) and the servant's acquired power (kasb). The act is created by God and acquired by the servant, so one act is under two powers without implying partnership.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main proof that one act can be under two powers?
A) Direct sensory experience.
B) Consensus that God empowers the servant and that God cannot empower over what He has no power over.
C) The Mu'tazila's admission.
D) Logical deduction from the concept of power.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of one act under two powers is proven by consensus and the principle that God only empowers over what He can do.

?
When you sin, do you blame it on God's decree or on yourself? Read the passage again: the act is under both powers. Does this change how you take responsibility for your sin? What will you change tonight?
?
If you truly believed that every act of yours is also under God's power, would you pray differently before making decisions? Will you now?
?
The Mu'tazila rely on imagination (wahm). Where in your life do you rely on imagination rather than revelation about Allah's power? Will you surrender that to the truth?

The Stone Example: Two Agents Moving One Object
Al-Nasafi uses the example of two men moving a stone to prove two agents can act on one object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi uses the stone example to refute the Mu'tazilite theory of generated acts and affirm that a single act can belong to two agents.

Core Idea
Two agents can act on one object—the stone example disproves the Mu'tazilite denial.

In this passage, al-Nasafi deploys a powerful argument against the Mu'tazilite concept of 'tawallud' (generated acts). By taking the simple case of two men moving a stone, he exposes an internal contradiction in the Mu'tazilite position and forces them into absurd evasions. The example becomes a test case for the broader question of whether a single act can be attributed to two agents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic and translation, then note the core argument: that if two men move a stone, the motion is an act of both, proving one act can be under two powers.
Pay attention to how al-Nasafi frames this as a necessary implication (ilzam).
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud
Recall that the Mu'tazilite theory of generated acts holds that acts are created by God but generated by humans. This leads to the problem of two agents acting on one object.
3
Follow the dialectical exchange with Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir
Bishr attempts to evade the implication by splitting the stone's motion among its parts. Note the counter-questions about odd numbers and equal agents.
This exchange illustrates the logical back-and-forth of kalam argumentation.
4
Conclude why the Mu'tazilite evasion fails
Bishr's claim that equal agents cannot be equal fails to resolve the underlying problem, as the scenario assumes equality to test the theory.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the trend shows the weakness of the Mu'tazilite position.

Then they, despite their aversion to the attachment of two powers to one act of power from two aspects, and the consensus of all rational persons on the impossibility of their attachment to it from the aspect of invention, their corrupt principles led them to permit their attachment to it from one aspect, namely invention. So the difficulty became great for them in that, and their leaders differed in seeking a way to escape from it, but they could not, and they gained nothing from that except ignorance and departure from the testimonies of knowledge. That is because the Ahl al-Sunna imposed upon them, on their theory of generation (tawallud), that if two men of equal power moved a stone and the stone moved, then what was in it of motion would be an act for the two movers and an object of power for both. Thus the single thing would be an act for two agents and an object of power for two powerful ones. It was said to him: If they were equal in constitution, strength, energy, and will, how can it be conceived that more is attributed to one and less to the other? He said: It is impossible for them to be equal in that. So it was said to him: Why did you say that? He said: Because if they were equal in that, it would necessitate one of two things: either the existence
Source pages p. 907

Commentary
This passage is an argument and refutation using the example of two men moving a stone. Al-Nasafi begins by noting that the Mu'tazilites, despite their aversion to two powers on one object from two aspects, were forced by their own corrupt principles to allow two powers from one aspect (invention). This created a serious difficulty for them. The Ahl al-Sunna presented a necessary implication (ilzām) based on the Mu'tazilite theory of generated acts (tawallud): if two equal men move a stone, the motion is an act of both and an object of power for both. This disproves the Mu'tazilite denial of one object for two powers and also refutes the Nadhāmiyya and Najjariyya who deny one act for two agents. In response, Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir tried to evade by splitting the stone's parts evenly. Al-Nasafi then records a dialectical exchange: if the parts are odd, Bishr assigns different numbers of motions. When asked how unequal attribution is possible if the men are equal in all respects, Bishr asserts that perfect equality is impossible, for it would lead to one of two absurdities (the sentence ends abruptly at the window boundary). The passage shows the Mu'tazilite failure to maintain their position against the stone example.

الإلزام
بشر بن المعتمر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bishr's splitting of the stone's motion among parts is a valid refutation of the Ahl al-Sunna argument.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that Bishr's solution fails when the agents are equal in all respects, as the unequal attribution becomes inexplicable and forces Bishr to deny the possibility of equality.

C) He argues that the stone's motion is generated by each independently.
D) He asserts that the men cannot be equal in power.

Key Takeaway
The stone example demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite theory of generated acts leads to the conclusion that one act can be under two powers, which they cannot consistently deny.

Refutation of the Objection that Two Agents Cannot Act on One Object
Nasafi refutes the objection that two agents cannot act on one object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the objection that two equal agents cannot act on a single object, and why joint action is possible.

Core Idea
Equality of agents does not make joint action impossible; only specific spatial constraints do.

This passage presents a classic objection in kalam against the possibility of two agents acting on one object, using an example of two men moving toward a center. Nasafi's response is layered, affirming divine power and logical consistency. We will examine both the objection and the refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its example
Start by reading the passage where the objector presents the scenario of two equal men trying to move toward a middle part. Note the two impossible outcomes he lists.
The objector uses this example to argue that joint action is impossible.
2
Identify the two impossible outcomes
The objector says either both men stay put or both arrive at the middle part. Both are considered impossible. Think about why each is impossible.
This is the core of the objection.
3
Understand Nasafi's first line of response
Nasafi begins by affirming that God can create equal agents, and their agreement on an act is not impossible. He challenges the assumption that equality prevents joint action.
He shifts the focus from equality to the nature of the act.
4
See the deeper point: occupation vs. joint action
Nasafi explains that the impossibility in the example is due to two bodies occupying one space, not to two agents moving one body. Joint action on a single object without occupation is possible.
This is the key distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والإرادة للتحرك إلى ذلك الجزء، لأنهما لو استويا ولا يكون أحدهما أولى ببلوغ مراده أدى إلى أحد أمرين كل واحد منهما محال فإنهما إما أن لا يبرحا من مكانهما من غير منع، وإما أن يوافيا ذلك الجزء جميعًا، وكل واحد من الأمرين فاسد، فكذا هذا. والجواب عن هذا أن يقال : لا وجه لإنكار قدرة الله تعالى أن يخلق شخصين مستويي القدرة والبنية، ومن ضرورة ذلك أن يكون في قدرة كل واحد منهما مثل ما في قدرة الآخر ليقع الفرق بين القوتين المستويتين والقوتين المختلفتين، ثم لا شك أن اتفاقهما على فعل ليس بممتنع ، وليس هذا الممكن يجعله محالاً أو من جعل ذلك المحال ممكنا لا مكان هذا، وهكذا هذا على كل من دفع موهومًا خوفا من وجوب المحال عليه؛ لأن دفع الممكن هربًا من المحال كتصحيح المحال هربًا من إحالة الصحيح. ثم نقول لهم: لا نزاع لأحد من العقلاء في كون ثبوت الرجلين المستويين في القوة واتفاقهما على الإرادة ممكنًا، ولا دليل في الفعل يدل على إحالته بل فيه دليل إمكانه، وكون فعل واحد لفاعلين وتعلق قدرتين بمقدور واحد مما للعقلاء فيه كلام، فهلا علمت بإفضاء ذلك الممكن إليه أنه ممكن حتى خالفت العقل والعقلاء، وجعلت الممكن ممتنعا، وهذا جهل فاحش. ثم نقول له: إنك سلمت أن تخليق الله رجلين مستويين في القوة والبنية من جملة الممكنات، وإنما جعلت المستحيل وجود إرادتهما لتحريك ذلك الجسم ويجوز وجود إرادة كل واحد منهما لتحريك الجسم والاعتماد عليه للتحريك على الانفراد فبعد ذلك نقول: إذا وجدت من أحدهما إرادة تحريك الحجر لماذا امتنع وجود إرادة تحريك ذلك الآخر، ولا مضادة بين الإرادتين والفعلين لتفرق محليهما، ولا فساد لمحل قدرة أحدهما عند ورود إرادة الآخر، فإن تعلق بالمثل الذي ضربه قلنا هناك : إنما استحال وجود إرادة الاثنين لأن إرادتهما للتحرك إلى الجزء المتوسط لا تقع إلا مع حكمها وهو التحرك، ولما استحال شغلهما ذلك الجزء المتوسط استحال وجود ما لا يقع إلا مع حركتيهما وهو الإرادة ولا استحالة في وجود الاعتماد من الاثنين على ذلك الجسم وتحريكهما إياه، فإن نوزعنا في كون إرادة التحرك التحرك مع نثبت ذلك بالدليل على أنا بنينا ذلك على أصلنا لدفع الإلزام للإلزام على الخصم وهو مستقيم، ولأن العلم باستحالة شغل اثنين جزءًا واحدًا من الممكن ضروري والعلم باستحالة دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين ليس بضروري لو كان لكان استدلاليًا وبإفضائه إلى جعل ما هو ممكن ممتنعا علم بطلان الاستدلال، وفيما استشهد به الأمر بخلافه، لأن ما علم ضرورة لا يتصور خطأه وبطلانه، فلا يمكن جعل أحدهما نظير للآخر، والله الموفق.
An act from two agents, or the division of the motion of an indivisible part, both are impossible, and that which leads to the impossible is itself impossible. Then he gave an example of this with a middle part between two men, one on his right and the other on his left: it is not permissible for the two men to be equal in strength and will to move toward that part, because if they were equal and neither of them is more entitled to achieve his aim, it would lead to one of two matters, each of which is impossible: either they do not leave their places without prevention, or they both reach that part together, and both matters are invalid, and so is this. The answer to this is to say: There is no basis for denying the power of God Almighty to create two persons equal in power and constitution, and from that it necessarily follows that in the power of each of them is like what is in the power of the other, so that the difference between equal powers and different powers occurs. Then there is no doubt that their agreement on an act is not impossible, and this possible thing is not made impossible, nor is that impossible made possible because of this. And likewise this applies to everyone who repels a mere imagination for fear of the necessity of the impossible upon him; because repelling the possible in flight from the impossible is like asserting the impossible in flight from making the sound unsound. Then we say to them: There is no dispute among rational people regarding the possibility of the existence of two men equal in strength and their agreement on will, and there is no proof in the act indicating its impossibility, rather there is proof of its possibility. And that one act from two agents and the attachment of two powers to one object is something about which rational people have discussion. So why did you not know that by that possible leading to it, that it is possible, until you contradicted reason and rational people, and made the possible impossible? This is gross ignorance. Then we say to him: You have conceded that God's creation of two men equal in strength and constitution is among the possibilities, and you only deemed impossible the existence of their will to move that body, while it is permissible for the will of each of them to exist individually to move the body and rely on it for movement. Then after that we say: If the will to move the stone exists from one of them, why is the existence of the will to move it from the other prevented? There is no opposition between the two wills and the two acts because their loci are separate, and no corruption of the locus of one's power upon the occurrence of the other's will. If he then cleaves to the example he gave, we say there: Indeed, the existence of the will of both is impossible because their will to move toward the middle part only occurs with its concomitant, which is movement, and since occupying that middle part by both is impossible, the existence of that which only occurs with their two movements—namely will—is impossible. But there is no impossibility in the existence of the reliance of the two on that body and their moving it. If we are contested regarding whether the will to move is movement, we prove that by evidence, based on our principle to rebut the objection against the opponent, and it is correct. And because the knowledge of the impossibility of two occupying one part is necessary by possibility, while the knowledge of the impossibility of one object of power falling under the power of two agents is not necessary—if it were, it would be inferential—and by its leading to making what is possible impossible, the invalidity of the inference is known. And in what he cited, the matter is contrary, because what is known necessarily cannot be conceived as erroneous or invalid, so one cannot be made analogous to the other. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 908

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. It begins by presenting an objection against the possibility of two agents acting on one object. The objector uses the example of two men equal in strength and will trying to move toward a middle part between them; if they are equal, then either they both remain stationary or both arrive, both impossible. Hence single act from two agents is impossible. Nasafi answers by first affirming God's power to create equal persons, and that their agreement on an act is not impossible. He then argues that the objector's example is flawed because it involves a necessary impossibility (two bodies occupying one space) which does not apply to two agents moving a single object without occupation. He elaborates that the will of each can exist separately without opposition, and that the impossibility in the example is due to the specific nature of occupying the middle part, not to the general idea of joint action. He concludes that the knowledge of the impossibility of two occupying one space is necessary, while the impossibility of two powers on one object is not necessary and is refuted by the fact that it leads to making the possible impossible.

Key Terms
فاعلين
جزء لا يتجزأ
إرادة
قدرة
إحالة
ممكن
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two agents are equal in strength and will, their joint action on a single object is impossible.
Correction: Equality does not prevent joint action; the impossibility in the example arises from the physical impossibility of two bodies occupying the same space, not from any inherent limitation on two agents acting on one object.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw Nasafi identifies in the objection about two men moving toward a middle part?
A) The objection assumes God cannot create equal persons.
B) The objection confuses the impossibility of two bodies occupying one space with the possibility of two agents acting on one body.
C) The objection ignores that will can be simultaneous without conflict.
D) The objection relies on a false analogy.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that joint action by two agents is possible, and the objector's impossibility results from a spatial constraint, not from the nature of joint agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you insist on a belief that contradicts clear evidence, are you like the objector who repels the possible out of fear of the impossible? In which area of your faith might you be doing this right now, and will you examine it honestly today?
?
Nasafi says that making the possible impossible is 'gross ignorance'. Where in your own thinking have you assumed something is impossible for God or in His creation when it is actually possible? What will you do to correct that assumption this week?
?
The objector's mistake was confusing a specific spatial impossibility with a general rule. Are there areas of your life where you take a particular, limited experience and wrongly generalize it into a universal truth about God or His ways? How can you break that pattern starting today?

Objection: Two agents moving a particle on a board
Nasafi presents an additional objection about two men moving a board with an indivisible particle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an additional objection that forces the opponent to admit that a single effect can result from the combined power of two agents.

Core Idea
Two equal agents acting together must share the effect equally.

This lesson presents a clever thought experiment that challenges the claim that only one agent can cause a given effect. Nasafi imagines two men moving a board with an indivisible particle to show that the particle's motion must be attributed to both equally. This forces the opponent to reconsider his position on shared causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the literal sense of the objection.
Notice that the passage is a single compressed sentence.
2
Understand the scenario
Picture the board, the indivisible particle (atom), and the two men grasping the two ends of the board. They move the board, displacing the particle.
The particle and board together form a single whole (fard).
3
Follow the logical necessity
Why must the motion be shared? Because the two men are equal in all respects (istiwa'), so there is no evidence (tarjih) to prefer one over the other. Therefore, the displacement cannot be attributed to only one.
This uses the principle of no preference without a preferrer.
4
See the objection's force
The opponent who denies that two agents can produce a single effect is forced to admit that in this case the motion is shared, contradicting his stance.
This is an ilzam (compelling argument).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألزموه أيضا أن جزءًا لا يتجزأ لو وضع على لوح وقبض على طرفي اللوح رجلان فحركاه فأزعج الجزء وكان الجزء مع أجزاء اللوح فردًا لابد أن حركة الجزء كانت بينهما لانعدام دليل ترجيح أحد الرجلين لاستوائهما في المعاني كلها.
And they also obligated him that if an indivisible part were placed on a board, and two men grasped the two ends of the board and moved it, thus displacing the part, and the part together with the parts of the board was a single entity, then necessarily the motion of the part was between them [shared], because of the absence of evidence for preferring one of the two men due to their equality in all respects.
Source pages p. 909

Commentary
This passage presents an additional objection (ilzam) against the opponent who denies that two agents can share an effect. The scenario: an indivisible particle is placed on a board; two men grasp opposite ends of the board and move it, thereby displacing the particle. Since the particle and the board together form a single whole, the particle's motion must be equally attributable to both men, as there is no reason to prefer one over the other. This forces the opponent to admit that a single effect (the particle's displacement) results from the power of two agents, contradicting his earlier stance.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ
لوح
رجلان
ترجيح
استواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the particle's motion is caused by only one agent, perhaps the one who pulls harder.
Correction: The objection assumes both men are equal in all respects, so there is no basis to prefer one. Hence the motion must be attributed to both equally.

Checkpoint
What does the objection about the board and particle demonstrate?
A) That an indivisible particle cannot exist.
B) That two agents can jointly produce a single motion.
C) That the board must be moved by one agent.
D) That the particle's motion is determined by its own nature.

Key Takeaway
This objection shows that when two agents are equal and act together, their effect must be jointly shared, undermining the claim that only one agent can cause a single effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve a success, do you ever pause to consider how many people (or circumstances) shared in making it happen? This passage shows that when agents are equal, the effect is unavoidably shared. Are you honest in attributing credit to others, or do you silently claim it all for yourself?
?
The objectors demand evidence before preferring one agent over another. In your judgments of others (e.g., who deserves praise or blame), do you rely on solid evidence or on assumptions? What would it change if you demanded the same level of evidence before giving yourself credit for a joint outcome?

The Contradiction of Indivisible Motion from Two Agents
Imam Nasafi explains the contradictory consequences of al-Murdar's view on motion and agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing a single indivisible motion to two agents leads to a logical contradiction, specifically that the same accident cannot be both obedience and disobedience.

Core Idea
One indivisible motion cannot be both obedience and disobedience to two agents.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi unpacks the implications of holding that motion is an indivisible accident but is produced by two agents. He shows that this leads to the absurd conclusion that the very same motion would have to be considered as obedience from one perspective and disobedience from another, which is impossible. He illustrates this with the example of wind moving something.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a general statement about accidents.
2
Identify the key premise about accidents
The author states that accidents do not divide in their essence but are divided by place, time, and agents. This is the foundation of the argument.
This premise is crucial for the reductio that follows.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
If motion is one indivisible accident and is attributed to two agents (one obedient, one disobedient), then the same motion would have to be both obedience and disobedience at once, which is impossible.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Consider the wind example
The author adds that even if one of the agents is wind, the same logical problem persists because the motion remains one thing.
This strengthens the argument by showing it applies broadly.
5
Reflect on the opponent's escape attempt
The author notes that the opponent's claim of ignorance does not rescue him from this consequence.
This closing remark reinforces the strength of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل واحد منهما لا [؟] في نفسها لأنها حركة واحدة وشيء واحد، والأعراض لا تنقسم في ذاتها، وإنما تنقسم بالمكان والزمان والفاعلين. ويلزمه جميع ما لزم المردار إذا كانت هي باعتبار ذاتها غير منقسمة فكانت هي باعتبار أحد الفاعلين طاعة وبالاعتبار الآخر معصية، وكذا إذا كان أحد المحركين ريحًا إذ هي في نفسها غير منقسمة فيلزمه جميع ما ألزمنا، ولم ينفعه هذا التجاهل.
Each one of them does not [divide?] in itself because it is one motion and one thing, and accidents do not divide in their essence, but they are divided by place, time, and agents. And everything that was incumbent on al-Murdar becomes incumbent on him: if it (the motion) is in its essence indivisible, then it would be in consideration of one agent obedience and in consideration of the other disobedience. Likewise if one of the moving [agents?] is wind, since it is in itself indivisible, then everything we have required becomes incumbent on him, and this ignorance did not benefit him.
Source pages p. 911

Commentary
This passage states a consequence of the view that motion is a single indivisible accident but is attributed to two agents. The author argues that if motion is indivisible in itself, then when it is considered as an act of obedience (if one agent is obedient) and as an act of disobedience (if the other is disobedient), the same thing would be both obedience and disobedience, which is impossible. Similarly, if one of the moving forces is wind, the same issue arises. The author concludes that the opponent's claim of ignorance does not save him from this consequence.

Key Terms
عرض
حركة
انقسام
فاعل
طاعة
معصية
مردار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that attributing an accident to different agents implies division in its essence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that division by agents does not entail division in the essence of the accident; the accident remains one thing in itself.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what follows from considering motion as one indivisible accident but attributed to two different agents?
A) It becomes two motions.
B) It would be both obedience and disobedience at the same time.
C) It is divided in essence.
D) It is divided by time and place only.

Key Takeaway
The indivisibility of an accident in its essence means it cannot bear contradictory attributes from different agents simultaneously.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you ever try to serve two masters—hoping it pleases God while also satisfying your own ego? How does this lesson expose the impossibility of that? What single intention will you purify today?
?
Think of a situation where you blamed someone else for your sin or credited yourself for a good deed. How does the indivisibility of the act force you to take full responsibility? What will you repent of right now?
?
If a single motion cannot be both obedience and disobedience, how does that challenge your excuses for mixed motives? Will you commit to examining your intentions before every action from now on? How will you start today?

The First Horn: Identical Portions
Nasafi presents the first horn of a dilemma about the division of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that each agent's portion is exactly the same leads to the conclusion that the motion is not truly divided, making the opponent's view indistinguishable from the view he opposes.

Core Idea
If portions are identical, there is no actual division.

Nasafi poses a dilemma to the opponent who says one motion is divided between two agents. The first possibility is that the portion of each agent is exactly the same. At first glance, this might seem to preserve the oneness of motion, but Nasafi shows that it leads to a contradiction: the very same act would then be both obedience and disobedience, and the motion would be undivided. This lesson examines that first horn.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening dilemma
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to the first possibility. Notice how Nasafi sets up the question: if motion is divided, is each portion the same or different?
The question forces the opponent to choose a side.
2
Understand the first possibility
The opponent might say the portions are identical. Nasafi argues that if the portions are the same, then the motion is undivided, and the act would be both obedience and disobedience, which is absurd. This shows that the opponent cannot escape the problem.
This horn reduces the opponent's view to the very view he is arguing against.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Consider why Nasafi compares this to the case where motion is said not to divide. If saying 'it does not divide' yields the same outcome as saying 'the portions are identical', then the opponent's division is meaningless.
The force of the argument is that the opponent's position collapses into self-contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نبين تجاهله فنقول له : إذا انقسمت الحركة على الفاعلين أكان قسم كل واحد منهما هو قسم صاحبه بعينه لا غير أم هو غير قسم صاحبه؟ فإن قال : هو قسم صاحبه، فإذا كان الفعل لهما لا على الانقسام، وكان على ما عليه قول خصومه من كونه طاعة ومعصية وفعلاً لفاعلين ومقدوراً لقادرين ولم يحصل الفرق بين انقسامها وامتناعها عن الانقسام، إذ لو قيل هي لا تنقسم لم يكن تحت هذا القول إلا هذا أن قسم كل واحد منهما هو قسم صاحبه. وإن قال: قسم كل واحد منهما غير قسم صاحبه في الذكر والعبارة إلا أن المنقسم واحد في الحقيقة. قيل : وخصومك يقولون : الكسب غير الخلق، وما عذب عليه غير ما لم يعذب عليه، وما قدر عليه الإنسان من الاكتساب غير ما لم يقدر عليه من الخلق في العبارة، والمخلوق هو المكتسب في الحقيقة، على هذا كان يتكلم حفص، فإن كان قولك هذا صحيحًا فقولهم كان صحيحا، وإن كان قولهم باطلاً كان قولك باطلاً. وإن أن أحد القسمين غير صاحبه على الحقيقة فهذا هو القول بتجزؤ الأعراض وانقسامها وهو لا يقول به، بل هو قول جعفر بن حرب ويقال له أيضا ما الفرق بينك وبين من يقول بتجزؤ الجزء فيجعل ما يلاقي الأرض غير ما يلاقي السماء، وما يلاقي الشمال غير ما يلاقي الجنوب، وما يلاقي الصبا غير ما يلاقي الدبور قياسًا على قسمتك الحركة الواحدة والشيء الواحد على مسببه لا في نفسه وهذا مما لا يجد فيه فرقا البتة.
Then we explain his ignorance and say to him: If motion is divided between the two agents, is the portion of each one exactly the portion of his fellow or is it different from his fellow's portion? If he says: It is his fellow's portion, then the act belongs to both without division, and it would be the same as his opponents' view that it is obedience and disobedience, an act of two agents, and a capacity of two power-holders, and no difference is obtained between its division and its refusal to divide. For if it is said it does not divide, there would be under this statement nothing except that the portion of each one is the portion of his fellow. And if he says: The portion of each one is different from his fellow's portion in mention and expression, but the thing divided is one in reality. He is told: Your opponents say that acquisition (al-kasb) is different from creation (al-khalq), and that for which one is punished is different from that for which one is not punished, and what man has power over by acquisition is different from what he does not have power over of creation in expression, while the created is the acquired in reality. That is how Hafs used to speak. If your statement is correct, theirs is correct; and if theirs is false, yours is false. And if one of the two portions is in reality different from the other, then this is the statement of the division of accidents and their partition, which he does not hold; rather it is the statement of Ja'far ibn Harb. And it is also said to him: What is the difference between you and one who says that a part is divisible, making that which faces the earth not what faces the sky, and that which faces the north not what faces the south, and that which faces the east wind not what faces the west wind, by analogy with your division of one motion and one thing according to its cause, not in itself? And this is something for which he can find no difference at all.
Source pages p. 911

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma to the opponent who claims that motion is one but divided between two agents. The author asks: Is the portion of each agent exactly the same portion or different? If the same, then the motion is not truly divided, and it reduces to the opponents' view that the same act is both obedience and disobedience, leading to contradiction. If they say the portions are different in expression but one in reality, the author compares it to the opponents' view of kasb and khalq: the statements are analogous, so if the opponents are false, so is this. If the portions are really different, then that entails the division of accidents, which the opponent rejects. Then the author draws an analogy to the division of an atom's parts based on direction, showing that the opponent's division is similarly unjustifiable.

Key Terms
انقسام
فاعل
قسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent might think that saying 'the portions are identical' saves the division, but Nasafi shows it collapses into undivided motion.
Correction: Identical portions mean no division, so the opponent's view becomes indistinguishable from the view that motion is not divided.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what happens if the opponent says the portion of each agent is exactly the same?
A) The motion is truly divided.
B) The act would be both obedience and disobedience.
C) The motion becomes two different motions.
D) The opponent wins the argument.

Key Takeaway
If the portions are identical, the motion is not divided, and the opponent's position collapses into the very view he opposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you ever subconsciously attribute it to your own ability rather than to Allah? How does this passage challenge you to recognize that your 'share' of the action is identical to His share, and that your action is not truly independent? What would change in your daily gratitude if you truly believed this?
?
Consider a sin you committed recently. Did you try to justify it by saying 'Shaytan made me do it' or 'I couldn't help it'? This passage shows that attributing the same act to two agents leads to absurdity. How does this expose the fallacy of shifting responsibility? Will you now take full ownership of your next mistake without excusing it?
?
If you truly believe that every action is willed by Allah, how does that affect your sense of personal accountability? Does it make you more or less responsible? This lesson pushes you to accept full ownership of your deeds while acknowledging Allah's sovereignty. How will you balance these two truths in your heart starting today?

The Second and Third Horns: Different Portions
Nasafi presents the second and third horns of the dilemma and concludes with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming the portions are different in expression but one in reality, or different in reality, leads to further contradictions and absurdities.

Core Idea
Different portions lead to either analogy with kasb/khalq or division of accidents.

Nasafi continues the dilemma. The opponent might try to say the portions are different in expression but one in reality, or that they are truly different. Nasafi shows that the first is analogous to the discredited view of kasb and khalq, while the second entails the division of accidents, which the opponent rejects. An analogy with dividing an atom's parts by direction underscores the absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the second possibility
Read the passage where the opponent says the portions are different in expression but one in reality. Nasafi compares this to the opponents' claim that kasb is different from khalq in expression but the created is the acquired in reality.
This analogy implies that if the opponents' view is false, then this is false too.
2
Examine the third possibility
If the opponent says the portions are truly different, then he falls into the statement of division of accidents, which he himself does not hold. Nasafi mentions that Ja'far ibn Harb holds this view.
The third horn is even more problematic for the opponent.
3
Understand the final analogy
Nasafi draws an analogy to someone who divides an atom's parts by direction, claiming that what faces the earth is different from what faces the sky. This shows the arbitrariness of dividing one thing based on external relations.
The opponent cannot find a difference to justify his division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نبين تجاهله فنقول له : إذا انقسمت الحركة على الفاعلين أكان قسم كل واحد منهما هو قسم صاحبه بعينه لا غير أم هو غير قسم صاحبه؟ فإن قال : هو قسم صاحبه، فإذا كان الفعل لهما لا على الانقسام، وكان على ما عليه قول خصومه من كونه طاعة ومعصية وفعلاً لفاعلين ومقدوراً لقادرين ولم يحصل الفرق بين انقسامها وامتناعها عن الانقسام، إذ لو قيل هي لا تنقسم لم يكن تحت هذا القول إلا هذا أن قسم كل واحد منهما هو قسم صاحبه. وإن قال: قسم كل واحد منهما غير قسم صاحبه في الذكر والعبارة إلا أن المنقسم واحد في الحقيقة. قيل : وخصومك يقولون : الكسب غير الخلق، وما عذب عليه غير ما لم يعذب عليه، وما قدر عليه الإنسان من الاكتساب غير ما لم يقدر عليه من الخلق في العبارة، والمخلوق هو المكتسب في الحقيقة، على هذا كان يتكلم حفص، فإن كان قولك هذا صحيحًا فقولهم كان صحيحا، وإن كان قولهم باطلاً كان قولك باطلاً. وإن أن أحد القسمين غير صاحبه على الحقيقة فهذا هو القول بتجزؤ الأعراض وانقسامها وهو لا يقول به، بل هو قول جعفر بن حرب ويقال له أيضا ما الفرق بينك وبين من يقول بتجزؤ الجزء فيجعل ما يلاقي الأرض غير ما يلاقي السماء، وما يلاقي الشمال غير ما يلاقي الجنوب، وما يلاقي الصبا غير ما يلاقي الدبور قياسًا على قسمتك الحركة الواحدة والشيء الواحد على مسببه لا في نفسه وهذا مما لا يجد فيه فرقا البتة.
Then we explain his ignorance and say to him: If motion is divided between the two agents, is the portion of each one exactly the portion of his fellow or is it different from his fellow's portion? If he says: It is his fellow's portion, then the act belongs to both without division, and it would be the same as his opponents' view that it is obedience and disobedience, an act of two agents, and a capacity of two power-holders, and no difference is obtained between its division and its refusal to divide. For if it is said it does not divide, there would be under this statement nothing except that the portion of each one is the portion of his fellow. And if he says: The portion of each one is different from his fellow's portion in mention and expression, but the thing divided is one in reality. He is told: Your opponents say that acquisition (al-kasb) is different from creation (al-khalq), and that for which one is punished is different from that for which one is not punished, and what man has power over by acquisition is different from what he does not have power over of creation in expression, while the created is the acquired in reality. That is how Hafs used to speak. If your statement is correct, theirs is correct; and if theirs is false, yours is false. And if one of the two portions is in reality different from the other, then this is the statement of the division of accidents and their partition, which he does not hold; rather it is the statement of Ja'far ibn Harb. And it is also said to him: What is the difference between you and one who says that a part is divisible, making that which faces the earth not what faces the sky, and that which faces the north not what faces the south, and that which faces the east wind not what faces the west wind, by analogy with your division of one motion and one thing according to its cause, not in itself? And this is something for which he can find no difference at all.
Source pages p. 911

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma to the opponent who claims that motion is one but divided between two agents. The author asks: Is the portion of each agent exactly the same portion or different? If the same, then the motion is not truly divided, and it reduces to the opponents' view that the same act is both obedience and disobedience, leading to contradiction. If they say the portions are different in expression but one in reality, the author compares it to the opponents' view of kasb and khalq: the statements are analogous, so if the opponents are false, so is this. If the portions are really different, then that entails the division of accidents, which the opponent rejects. Then the author draws an analogy to the division of an atom's parts based on direction, showing that the opponent's division is similarly unjustifiable.

Key Terms
كسب
خلق
حفص
جعفر بن حرب
تجزؤ الأعراض

Checkpoint

A) It is the correct solution.
B) It is analogous to the kasb/khalq distinction of his opponents.
C) It implies the division of accidents.
D) It is a view held by Ja'far ibn Harb.

Key Takeaway
The second and third horns show that any attempt to divide motion between two agents leads to contradictions or untenable metaphysical commitments.

Inconsistency in the Division of Accidents
Nasafi exposes an internal contradiction in the opponent's division of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi exposes an internal inconsistency in the opponent's division of accidents by place, time, and agents.

Core Idea
Consistency demands that if accidents divide by place and time, then divine speech and persisting accidents must also divide.

Nasafi’s argument here is a classic move in kalam: he takes the opponent’s own principle and shows that it leads to a contradiction when applied consistently. The opponent claims that accidents are divided by place, time, and agents—meaning that an accident in one place is different from that in another. But then he makes an exception for the divine speech and for accidents that subsist across two times. Nasafi points out that this is inconsistent. In this lesson, we will examine the precise nature of this inconsistency and its implications for the opponent’s position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Read the passage and identify the opponent's position: accidents are divided by place, time, and agents, so that accidents in different places or times are different.
This is the principle that the opponent accepts.
2
Identify the two exceptions
The opponent then makes two exceptions: first, the divine speech (al-kalam al-wahid) resides in many places without differing; second, persisting accidents exist at two times without differing. Find these exceptions in the text.
The opponent’s claim about subsistence (baqa') is key here.
3
Recognize the inconsistency
Nasafi argues that if the opponent applies the division principle to some accidents, he must apply it consistently to all, including divine speech and persisting accidents. Failure to do so is a contradiction (nāqiḍ). Understand why this is a logical flaw.
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنه ناقض حيث زعم أن الأعراض تنقسم باعتبار الأمكنة والأزمنة والفاعلين فيكون ما وجد منها في مكان غير ما وجد في غيره من الأمكنة، وما وجد منها في زمان غير ما وجد منها في غيره من الأزمنة. ثم زعم أن الكلام الواحد يحل في أماكن كثيرة متغايرة ولم يجعله متغايرا بتغاير الأمكنة، وزعم أن أكثر الأعراض توجد في وقتين لأنه يقول ببقائها، ولم يجعل تغاير الأزمنة موجبًا تغاير ما فيها من الأعراض، والله الموفق.
Then he is inconsistent: he claimed that accidents are divided by consideration of places, times, and agents, so that what occurs in one place is different from what occurs in other places, and what occurs in one time is different from what occurs in other times. Then he claimed that the one speech (al-kalam al-wahid) resides in many different places but did not make it different due to the difference of places. And he claimed that most accidents exist at two times because he holds their subsistence (baqa'), but he did not make the difference of times necessitate the difference of the accidents in them. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 912

Commentary
This passage points out an inconsistency in the opponent's position. On one hand, he asserts that accidents are divided according to places, times, and agents, meaning that an accident in one place is different from that in another place. On the other hand, he claims that the single divine speech (al-kalam al-wahid) can exist in many different places without being affected by the difference of places. Similarly, he claims that most accidents exist at two times (because they subsist), but he does not hold that the difference of times makes the accidents different. The author presents this as a contradiction in the opponent's own principles.

D) Claiming that motion is divided by agents but not by place.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that the opponent’s division of accidents by place and time is inconsistent if he makes exceptions for divine speech and persisting accidents.

Refutation of Ja'far ibn Harb on Motion Division
Nasafi refutes Ja'far's claim about motion division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unity of time, place, and agent does not entail that an act is one, and how Nasafi uses the example of color and taste to refute Ja'far ibn Harb's division of motion.

Core Idea
Unity of agent, time, and place does not unify distinct effects.

Nasafi confronts Ja'far ibn Harb's claim that each part's motion is actually two halves from two different agents. He exposes the flaw by asking: if that logic held, then a motion by a single agent would also be two acts. The opponent might reply that the motion is one because its time, place, and agent are one. Nasafi refutes this by pointing out that distinct things (like a color and a taste) can coexist in the same time and place from the same agent, so unity of those factors does not guarantee unity of the act itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice that the argument is cut off at the end but the main refutation is clear.
The Arabic text ends mid-sentence; the argument continues beyond this page.
2
Identify Ja'far's claim and the objection
Ja'far claims motion is divided into two halves, each from a different agent. The first objection asks: why not say a single agent's motion is also two acts? This forces the opponent to find a criterion for unity.
3
Examine the refutation example
Nasafi uses the example of God creating a color and a taste at the same time in an indivisible place. Despite unity of agent, time, and place, they remain two distinct things. This shows that division does not require multiple agents.
This directly undermines Ja'far's reasoning that multiple agents cause division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم جعفر بن حرب المعروف بالأشج: أن حركة كل جزء منقسمة قسمين على الحقيقة نصفها لهذا الدافع ونصفها لذلك وهي ذات بعضين متغايرين حقيقة. فقيل له: ما أنكرت أن تكون الحركة التي يفعلها واحد فعلين متغايرين أيضا. أنها فعل واحد غير منقسم لانعدام علة انقسامها لأن زمانها ومكانها وفاعلها واحد. قيل له: هذا جهل، لأن ما يتغاير في نفسه لا أثر لاتحاد الزمان والمكان والفاعل في اتحاده كما لو خلق الله تعالى في زمان واحد في جزء لا يتجزأ من الأمكنة لونًا وطعما كانا شيئين متغايرين، وإن اتحد الفاعل والمكان والزمان، فلو كانت الحركة نصفين متغايرين عند تعدد الفاعلين كانت أيضًا نصفين متغايرين عند
And Ja'far ibn Harb, known as al-Ashajj, claimed that the motion of every part is divided into two halves in reality: half of it is from this pusher and half from that, and it is a thing having two different parts in reality. So it was said to him: What prevents you from saying that the motion that one agent does is two different acts as well? It is one indivisible act due to the absence of a cause for its division, because its time, place, and agent are one. He was told: This is ignorance, because what is different in itself is not affected by the unity of time, place, and agent in its unity — just as if God created in a single time in an indivisible part of places a color and a taste, they would be two different things, even though the agent, place, and time are one. So if motion were two halves when the agents are multiple, it would also be two halves when...
Source pages p. 912

Commentary
This passage presents Ja'far ibn Harb's claim that each part's motion is actually two halves, one from each agent. The author then raises an objection: by the same logic, why not say that a motion done by a single agent is two different acts? The opponent might respond that it is one because the time, place, and agent are unified. The author refutes this by pointing out that the unity of time, place, and agent does not entail unity of the thing itself; he gives the example of God creating a color and a taste in the same instant and indivisible place, which are two distinct things despite unity of agent, time, and place. Therefore, if motion is divided into two halves when agents are multiple, it would also be divided into two halves when the agent is one, which leads to absurdity. The argument is cut off but the point is clear.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If time, place, and agent are one, then the act must be one and indivisible.
Correction: The example of color and taste shows that distinct entities can coexist in the same time, place, and from the same agent, so unity of those factors does not guarantee unity of the act.
