D) A sound and a color created in different places.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes Ja'far's division of motion by showing that distinct effects can coexist in the same time, place, and from the same agent, so dividing motion does not require multiple agents.

?

In your own life, when you do one good deed, do you assume it has only one spiritual effect? This lesson reminds you that God creates distinct realities simultaneously. Will you today, in your dhikr or salah, deliberately reflect on the multiplicity of divine blessings in a single moment?

Commit now to write down the multiple effects of one action you regret, and seek forgiveness for each aspect.
?

Have you ever combined distinct spiritual states (like pride and charity) in your heart, assuming they are one? Pause and ask yourself: is there an area where you conflate multiple intentions or motives in a single act, assuming God sees them as one? Refutation of Division of the Part by Directions
Nasafi refutes a false analogy for dividing the part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi rejects the analogy between dividing a part by directions and dividing motion by its cause.

Core Idea
A part cannot be divided by directions; motion is divided only in its cause, not in itself.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles an opponent who claims that a physical part is divisible by the different directions it faces—earth, sky, etc. The opponent draws an analogy with motion, arguing that just as a single motion is divided according to its cause, so too the part is divided in itself. Nasafi dismisses this analogy as flawed and points out its failure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand Nasafi's refutation. Notice how the opponent divides the part by directions.
2
Identify the opponent's analogy
The opponent compares dividing the part by directions to dividing motion by its cause. Understand why he thinks this works.
3
Recognize Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi argues that motion is divided only in its cause, not in itself. Ask yourself why this makes the analogy invalid for the part.
The key is that the part is being divided in itself, while motion is divided only in its cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بينك وبين من يقول بتجزؤ الجزء فيجعل ما يلاقي الأرض غير ما يلاقي السماء، وما يلاقي الشمال غير ما يلاقي الجنوب، وما يلاقي الصبا غير ما يلاقي الدبور قياسًا على قسمتك الحركة الواحدة والشيء الواحد على مسببه لا في نفسه وهذا مما لا يجد فيه فرقا البتة. ثم إنه ناقض حيث زعم أن الأعراض تنقسم باعتبار الأمكنة والأزمنة والفاعلين فيكون ما وجد منها في مكان غير ما وجد في غيره من الأمكنة، وما وجد منها في زمان غير ما وجد منها في غيره من الأزمنة. ثم زعم أن الكلام الواحد يحل في أماكن كثيرة متغايرة ولم يجعله متغايرا بتغاير الأمكنة، وزعم أن أكثر الأعراض توجد في وقتين لأنه يقول ببقائها، ولم يجعل تغاير الأزمنة موجبًا تغاير ما فيها من الأعراض، والله الموفق.
The difference between you and one who says that the part is divisible, making what faces the earth other than what faces the sky, and what faces the north other than what faces the south, and what faces the east wind other than what faces the west wind, by analogy with your division of a single motion and a single thing according to its cause, not in itself—and he finds no difference in this at all. Then he contradicted when he claimed that accidents are divided with regard to places, times, and agents, so that what exists among them in one place is other than what exists in other places, and what exists among them in one time is other than what exists in other times. Then he claimed that a single speech (al-kalam al-wahid) subsists in many different places and did not make it different due to the difference of places, and he claimed that most accidents exist in two times because he asserts their subsistence, and he did not make the difference of times necessitating the difference of what is in them of accidents. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 912

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent who claims that a physical part (الجزء) is divisible by virtue of its different directions (e.g., facing earth vs. sky). The opponent draws an analogy with motion, arguing that just as a single motion is divided according to its cause (e.g., into push and pull), so too the part is divided in itself by its different aspects. Nasafi rejects this analogy, stating that motion is divided only with respect to its cause, not in its own essence, and that the opponent finds no real difference. Moreover, Nasafi points out a contradiction in the opponent's position: the opponent claims that accidents (الأعراض) are divided by places, times, and agents, but when it comes to speech (الكلام), he claims that a single speech subsists in many different places without being divided by place. Similarly, he claims that most accidents subsist for two times (because he believes in their endurance), but he does not make the difference of times cause a division of those accidents. Thus, the opponent's own principles are inconsistent.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The part can be divided by directions in the same way that motion is divided by its cause.
Correction: Nasafi shows that motion is divided only in its cause, not in itself, so the analogy does not apply to the part.

Key Takeaway

Contradiction in the Opponent's View on Accidents and Speech
Nasafi exposes contradictory reasoning in the opponent's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi exposes an internal contradiction in the opponent's treatment of accidents and speech.

Core Idea
The opponent claims accidents are divided by place and time, but makes an exception for speech, revealing inconsistency.

Nasafi continues his refutation by highlighting a contradiction in the opponent's own principles. The opponent asserts that accidents (like motion) are divided by places, times, and agents. Yet when it comes to speech (al-kalam), he claims that a single speech can exist in many places without being divided. Similarly, he claims that most accidents endure for two times, but does not allow the difference in times to necessitate a difference in the accidents themselves. This inconsistency undermines his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the two inconsistencies Nasafi points out.
2
Identify the first inconsistency
The opponent says accidents are divided by places, but claims a single speech exists in many places without being divided. Why is this contradictory?
Nasafi uses the opponent's own principle against him.
3
Identify the second inconsistency
The opponent says most accidents exist in two times (because they endure), but does not consider time differences as causing division.
This shows the opponent is inconsistent in applying his own rule about times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بينك وبين من يقول بتجزؤ الجزء فيجعل ما يلاقي الأرض غير ما يلاقي السماء، وما يلاقي الشمال غير ما يلاقي الجنوب، وما يلاقي الصبا غير ما يلاقي الدبور قياسًا على قسمتك الحركة الواحدة والشيء الواحد على مسببه لا في نفسه وهذا مما لا يجد فيه فرقا البتة. ثم إنه ناقض حيث زعم أن الأعراض تنقسم باعتبار الأمكنة والأزمنة والفاعلين فيكون ما وجد منها في مكان غير ما وجد في غيره من الأمكنة، وما وجد منها في زمان غير ما وجد منها في غيره من الأزمنة. ثم زعم أن الكلام الواحد يحل في أماكن كثيرة متغايرة ولم يجعله متغايرا بتغاير الأمكنة، وزعم أن أكثر الأعراض توجد في وقتين لأنه يقول ببقائها، ولم يجعل تغاير الأزمنة موجبًا تغاير ما فيها من الأعراض، والله الموفق.
The difference between you and one who says that the part is divisible, making what faces the earth other than what faces the sky, and what faces the north other than what faces the south, and what faces the east wind other than what faces the west wind, by analogy with your division of a single motion and a single thing according to its cause, not in itself—and he finds no difference in this at all. Then he contradicted when he claimed that accidents are divided with regard to places, times, and agents, so that what exists among them in one place is other than what exists in other places, and what exists among them in one time is other than what exists in other times. Then he claimed that a single speech (al-kalam al-wahid) subsists in many different places and did not make it different due to the difference of places, and he claimed that most accidents exist in two times because he asserts their subsistence, and he did not make the difference of times necessitating the difference of what is in them of accidents. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 912

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent who claims that a physical part (الجزء) is divisible by virtue of its different directions (e.g., facing earth vs. sky). The opponent draws an analogy with motion, arguing that just as a single motion is divided according to its cause (e.g., into push and pull), so too the part is divided in itself by its different aspects. Nasafi rejects this analogy, stating that motion is divided only with respect to its cause, not in its own essence, and that the opponent finds no real difference. Moreover, Nasafi points out a contradiction in the opponent's position: the opponent claims that accidents (الأعراض) are divided by places, times, and agents, but when it comes to speech (الكلام), he claims that a single speech subsists in many different places without being divided by place. Similarly, he claims that most accidents subsist for two times (because he believes in their endurance), but he does not make the difference of times cause a division of those accidents. Thus, the opponent's own principles are inconsistent.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's view on accidents and speech is consistent.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the opponent's claim that accidents are divided by places and times is contradicted by his treatment of speech and enduring accidents.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi say the opponent claims about speech?
A) Speech is divided by places.
B) A single speech exists in many places without being divided.
C) Speech is not an accident.
D) Speech is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi exposes the opponent's inconsistency by showing that the same principles applied to accidents are not applied to speech and enduring accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If the divine speech is one yet present in multiple copies of the Quran, what does that demand of you when you recite or hear any verse? How does the unity of speech call you to a deeper reverence, not familiarity?
?
Nasafi shows that the opponent's view breaks down under scrutiny. What part of your faith have you never subjected to such scrutiny, perhaps out of comfort? Are you willing to let the truth expose your inconsistencies, even if it is uncomfortable?

Refutation of Ja'far ibn Harb's Division of Motion
Nasafi refutes Ja'far ibn Harb's claim that motion is divided into two halves when two agents act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unity of agent, time, and place does not necessitate unity of the effect, as demonstrated in Nasafi's refutation of Ja'far ibn Harb.

Core Idea
A thing's intrinsic diversity is not erased by unity of external factors.

Ja'far ibn Harb argued that when two agents push a body, each part's motion is actually two halves. Nasafi refutes this by showing that if motion were intrinsically divisible, it would be divisible even with one agent. The refutation uses a powerful analogy: color and taste created simultaneously in one atom remain distinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: claim, objection, response, refutation.
The argument is tightly reasoned; follow each step.
2
Identify the main claim and refutation
Ja'far's claim: motion is divided into two halves when two agents push. Nasafi's refutation: if that were true, motion would also be divided with one agent. The opponent admits it is not.
This is a parity argument.
3
Analyze the analogy
Nasafi uses the example of God creating color and taste in the same indivisible point of space at the same time. They remain two distinct things despite unity of agent, place, time.
The analogy proves that intrinsic diversity persists regardless of external factors.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since motion is not divided when one agent acts, it cannot be divided when agents are multiple. The original claim is false.
This refutes Ja'far's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم جعفر بن حرب المعروف بالأشج: أن حركة كل جزء منقسمة قسمين على الحقيقة نصفها لهذا الدافع ونصفها لذلك وهي ذات بعضين متغايرين حقيقة. فقيل له: ما أنكرت أن تكون الحركة التي يفعلها واحد فعلين متغايرين أيضا. أنها فعل واحد غير منقسم لانعدام علة انقسامها لأن زمانها ومكانها وفاعلها واحد. قيل له: هذا جهل، لأن ما يتغاير في نفسه لا أثر لاتحاد الزمان والمكان والفاعل في اتحاده كما لو خلق الله تعالى في زمان واحد في جزء لا يتجزأ من الأمكنة لونًا وطعما كانا شيئين متغايرين، وإن اتحد الفاعل والمكان والزمان، فلو كانت الحركة نصفين متغايرين عند تعدد الفاعلين كانت أيضًا نصفين متغايرين عند اتحاد الفاعل، وحيث لم يكن كذلك عند اتحاد الفاعل دل أنه ليس كذلك عند تعدد الفاعلين.
And Ja'far ibn Harb, known as al-Ashajj, claimed: that the motion of every part is divided into two halves in reality—half of it is for this pusher and half for that—and it is a thing having two different parts in reality. So it was said to him: What prevents you from [saying] that the motion which one [agent] performs is also two different acts? [He said:] It is a single act, indivisible, due to the absence of a cause for its division, because its time, place, and agent are one. It was said to him: This is ignorance, because what is different in itself is not affected by the unity of time, place, and agent in its being one—just as if God Almighty creates in one time, in an indivisible part of space, a color and a taste, they would be two different things, even though the agent, place, and time are one. So if motion were two different halves when agents are multiple, it would also be two different halves when the agent is one. And since it is not so when the agent is one, it indicates that it is not so when agents are multiple.
Source pages p. 912

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the claim of Ja'far ibn Harb (known as al-Ashajj) that the motion of each part is actually divided into two halves, each half belonging to a different agent when two agents push the body. The refutation begins by asking why, then, a motion produced by a single agent is not also divided into two acts. Ja'far responds that when there is one agent, the motion is one and indivisible because its time, place, and agent are unified. Nasafi rejects this as ignorance, arguing that real diversity in a thing is not affected by the unity of external factors such as time, place, and agent. He gives the example of God creating color and taste simultaneously in the same indivisible point of space: they remain two distinct things despite the unity of agent, place, and time. Thus, by parity of reasoning, if motion were truly two halves when agents are multiple, it would also be two halves when the agent is one. Since the opponent admits it is not divided when one agent, the premise that it is divided when multiple agents is false.

Key Terms
جعفر بن حرب
الأشج
حركة
جزء لا يتجزأ
لون
طعم
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity of agent, time, and place necessarily makes an act single and indivisible.
Correction: Nasafi shows that a thing's intrinsic diversity is independent of external factors; color and taste remain two even when created simultaneously in one place by one agent.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument against Ja'far ibn Harb's division of motion?
A) Motion is an accident and cannot be divided.
B) If motion were divided when agents are multiple, it would also be divided when the agent is one.
C) The body would not move if motion were divided.
D) Two agents cannot produce a single motion.

Key Takeaway
Motion's division is not caused by multiplicity of agents; intrinsic divisibility would persist regardless of external unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever assumed that because something in your life came from a single source (e.g., a trial, a blessing), it must be simple or have a single purpose? How does this lesson's principle—that intrinsic diversity is not erased by external unity—challenge that assumption? Will you now examine the internal complexity of events you previously treated as one-dimensional?
?
The analogy of color and taste shows that God can create two distinct realities in the same instant. Do you ever reduce God's actions to a single 'motive' or attribute, forgetting that His wisdom and mercy can produce multiple distinct outcomes in one event? How will you remind yourself to see the richness of His actions rather than oversimplify them?

The Half-Motion Dilemma
Nasafi questions whether half-motion causes the body to move, showing both answers lead to contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses a two-branch argument to show that the concept of half-motion is self-contradictory.

Core Idea
Half-motion cannot both be insufficient and cause movement.

Nasafi now presents a logical dilemma to the opponent who claims that each agent produces a half-motion. He asks: does the half-motion actually cause the body to move? The answer leads to contradictions in either case. This passage is a classic example of dialectical refutation in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: a question with two possible answers.
2
Identify the two branches
The argument splits into two branches: if the opponent says 'yes' (half-motion causes movement) and if he says 'no' (it does not). Find these in the text.
Each branch leads to a contradiction.
3
Understand the 'yes' branch
If half-motion causes movement, then each agent's half-motion is itself sufficient to move the body, meaning each agent produces a full motion. This contradicts the opponent's claim of half-motion.
The opponent's doctrine is that each agent only produces a half-motion, not a full motion.
4
Understand the 'no' branch
If half-motion does NOT cause movement, then it is not a real motion. Two such non-motions combined still do not cause movement, yet the body does move. This contradicts empirical observation.
The opponent cannot deny that the body moves when both agents push.
5
See the overall refutation
Both answers lead to contradictions, so the opponent's position is untenable. This is a powerful dialectical refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: أيزول الجسم بنصف الحركة التي حصلها واحد منهما؟ فإن قال: نعم. فهو إذا حرَّكه فكان وجد بكل واحد من المحركين حركة على حدة وحصل لكل محرك حركة على حدة، وهذا ليس بمذهبه. وإن قال: كل نصف ليس بحركة فإذا ليس كل نصف بزوال النصفان إذا اجتمعا فقد اجتمع ما ليس بزوال وما ليس بزوال وباجتماع ما ليس بزوال وما ليس بزوال وما ليس بحركة وما ليس بحركة لا يزول الجسم عن المكان الأول، وإذا زال دل على وجود الزوال.
Then it is said to him: Does the body move (remove from its place) by the half-motion that one of them obtains? If he says: Yes. Then when he moves it, there would exist for each of the two movers a motion separately, and for each mover a motion would be obtained separately, and this is not his doctrine. If he says: Each half is not a motion, then each half is not a removal (زوال). The two halves, when they combine, would have combined what is not a removal and what is not a removal. And by the combination of what is not a removal and what is not a removal and what is not a motion—what is not a motion does not remove the body from its first place. And if it does remove, it indicates the existence of removal (زوال).
Source pages p. 913

Commentary
This passage is a further objection against Ja'far ibn Harb's claim that each agent produces a half-motion. The questioner asks: does the body move (i.e., leave its place) by the half-motion produced by one agent? If the opponent answers yes, then that half-motion itself is sufficient to move the body, which means each agent independently produces a full motion (since each half-motion would cause movement separately). That contradicts the opponent's claim that each only produces a half-motion. If the opponent answers no, then each half is not a motion (since motion is defined as that which causes movement). Then the two halves combined are simply two non-motions, and two non-motions cannot produce motion. Yet empirically the body does move when both agents push, so the opponent's position is self-contradictory.

Key Terms
نصف الحركة
زوال
حركة
محرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a half-motion can be a motion and cause movement while still being a half.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if it causes movement, it is a full motion; if it does not, it is not a motion at all. Half-motion is thus an incoherent concept.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi conclude if the opponent says half-motion does not cause movement?
A) Then each half is a full motion.
B) Then two non-motions cannot cause movement, contradicting observation.
C) Then the body moves by both halves together.
D) Then the half-motion is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The half-motion hypothesis is self-contradictory because it cannot consistently explain how movement occurs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a logical dilemma in your own beliefs, do you try to reconcile both sides like the half-motion theorist, or do you accept the consequences of one clear truth even if it means giving up a comfortable middle ground? What would it take for you to choose the latter today?
?
This passage shows that two non-motions cannot combine to produce motion. In your life, are there areas where you expect good results from a combination of insufficient or half-hearted efforts? How does this lesson call you to seek a single, genuine cause—namely, sincere reliance on Allah—rather than patchwork solutions?
?
If even the smallest physical effect cannot arise from non-causes, what does that imply about your need for a single, true Source of change in your character and repentance? Will you stop attributing spiritual growth to a mix of your own efforts and divine grace as if they were two half-causes, and instead acknowledge that all true transformation comes from Allah alone?

Half-Motion and the Absurdity of Many Movers
Nasafi objects that half-motion leads to coexistence of stillness and motion, and many movers make motion absurdly fractional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that half-motion leads to a contradiction between stillness and motion, and that many movers lead to absurd fractional motions.

Core Idea
Stillness cannot coexist with any part of motion; many movers fragment motion absurdly.

This lesson examines Nasafi's final objection against the idea that two agents contribute half-motion each. He shows that this leads to an impossible coexistence of stillness and motion, and that if extended to many agents, motion would be divided into absurdly small fractions. This reinforces the Maturidi position on the impossibility of multiple divine agents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Focus on the logical dilemma presented.
Pay attention to the two-part structure: first the half-motion objection, then the many-movers consequence.
2
Analyze the dilemma
Trace the conditional reasoning: if half-motion moves the body, then it is actually full motion; if not, then stillness coexists with some motion, which is impossible.
Nasafi uses the impossibility of a thing coexisting with part of its opposite.
3
Consider the broader implication
Nasafi extends the logic to many agents: motion would be divided into fractions like tenths and sixths, which is absurd. Reflect on why this refutes the opponent's position.
This shows the opponent's view leads to irrational consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: لو أوجد أحدهما في كل جزء من أجزاء الجسم نصف الحركة الذي هو حصته أيزول الجسم عن مكانه الأول؟ فإن قال: نعم. فهو إذا حركة على ما بينا، وإن قال: لا. فقد بقي في المكان الأول وبقي ساكنا فيه فكان فيه اجتماع السكون وبعض الحركة ويستحيل اجتماع الشيء مع بعض ما يضاده، ثم النصف الآخر أيضا ليس بحركة فيجوز أن يجتمع معه السكون أيضا إذ السكون قد يجامع ما ليس بحركة وما ليس بحركة كما يجامع اللون والطعم، وهذا كله تجاهل وخروج عن المعارف. ومقتضى هذا أن جماعة كثيرة لو حركوا جسمًا ينبغي أن تحصل حركة كل جزء متبعضة على عدد الفاعلين، فتكون كل حركة منقسمة إلى الأعشار والأسداس وأكثر، وهذا جهل فاحش.
Then it is said to him: If one of them produces in every part of the body's parts the half-motion which is his share, does the body move from its first place? If he says: Yes. Then it is motion according to what we explained. If he says: No. Then it remains in the first place and remains still in it, and there would occur in it the coexistence of stillness and some motion, and it is impossible for a thing to coexist with a part of its opposite. Then the other half is also not a motion, so it is possible for stillness to coexist with it, since stillness sometimes coexists with what is not a motion and what is not a motion, just as it coexists with color and taste. All of this is ignorance and departure from knowledge. And the consequence of this is that if a large group moved a body, the motion of each part would have to be fragmented according to the number of agents, so that every motion would be divided into tenths, sixths, and more. This is gross ignorance.
Source pages p. 913

Commentary
In this final objection, the questioner considers the scenario where each agent produces half-motion in every part of the body. The question: does the body move from its place? If yes, then the half-motion is indeed a full motion as argued previously. If no, then the body remains still, which means stillness coexists with a part of motion—i.e., with some motion. But stillness (sukun) is opposite to motion (haraka), and it is impossible for a thing to coexist with part of its opposite (i.e., stillness cannot be present with any motion). Moreover, the other half from the second agent is also not a motion, so stillness could in principle coexist with it as well, but that leads to an absurd situation where the body is still despite two agents acting. Nasafi dismisses this as ignorance. He then points out the broader implication: if each agent provides only a half- or fractional motion per part, then if many agents (e.g., a crowd) push a body, the motion in each part would be divided into as many fractions as there are agents—tenths, sixths, etc.—which is clearly contrary to reason and observation.

Key Terms
نصف الحركة
سكون
حركة
مضاد
جماعة كثيرة
أعشار
أسداس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Stillness can coexist with a part of motion.
Correction: Nasafi argues that stillness (sukun) is opposite to motion (haraka), and it is impossible for a thing to coexist with even a part of its opposite.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does Nasafi derive from the opponent's position?
A) Motion would be infinitely divisible.
B) Motion would be divided into fractions like tenths and sixths if many agents act.
C) The body would not move at all.
D) The body would move in two directions.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that the opponent's view leads to a logical contradiction (stillness with part of motion) and an absurd consequence (fractional motion from many agents), thereby refuting the possibility of multiple divine agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you ever entertained the possibility of multiple creators, how does Nasafi's argument about stillness and motion expose the logical impossibility of that belief? What does this mean for your certainty that only Allah creates and sustains all motion?
?

?
Nasafi says the opponent's view leads to 'ignorance and departure from knowledge.' Are there areas in your own understanding of Allah's actions where you might be unknowingly falling into a similar error? What steps will you take to root out such false assumptions?

The Impossibility of Composite Motion: The Initial Argument
Nasafi presents a logical dilemma against the divisibility of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's logical argument against the idea that motion can be divided into parts.

Core Idea
If half a motion is not a motion, two halves cannot produce motion.

In this passage, Nasafi presents a dilemma to those who claim motion is composed of parts. He shows that both accepting or denying that half a motion causes motion leads to absurdities, ultimately proving that motion is indivisible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully and its translation. Note the logical structure.
2
Identify the dilemma
Find the key question: does the body move with half the motion produced by one agent? Consider both answers.
3
Follow the consequences
Trace the implications of each answer: if yes, each half is a full motion; if no, halves are non-motions.
This leads to contradictions.
4
Understand the final absurdity
If many agents move a body, motion would be fragmented into countless fractions, which is obviously false.
This refutes the divisibility claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتحاد الفاعل، وحيث لم يكن كذلك عند اتحاد الفاعل دل أنه ليس كذلك عند تعدد الفاعلين. ثم يقال له: أيزول الجسم بنصف الحركة التي حصلها واحد منهما؟ فإن قال: نعم. فهو إذا حَرَّكه فكان وجد بكل واحد من المحركين حركة على حدة وحصل لكل محرك حركة على حدة، وهذا ليس بمذهبه. وإن قال: كل نصف ليس بحركة فإذا ليس كل نصف بزوال النصفان إذا اجتمعا فقد اجتمع ما ليس بزوال وما ليس بزوال وباجتماع ما ليس بزوال وما ليس بزوال وما ليس بحركة وما ليس بحركة لا يزول الجسم عن المكان الأول، وإذا زال دل على وجود الزوال. ثم يقال له: لو أوجد أحدهما في كل جزء من أجزاء الجسم نصف الحركة الذي هو حصته أيزول الجسم عن مكانه الأول؟ فإن قال: نعم. فهو إذا حركة على ما بينا، وإن قال: لا. فقد بقي في المكان الأول وبقي ساكنا فيه فكان فيه اجتماع السكون وبعض الحركة ويستحيل اجتماع الشيء مع بعض ما يضاده، ثم النصف الآخر أيضا ليس بحركة فيجوز أن يجتمع معه السكون أيضا إذ السكون قد يجامع ما ليس بحركة وما ليس بحركة كما يجامع اللون والطعم، وهذا كله تجاهل وخروج عن المعارف. ومقتضى هذا أن جماعة كثيرة لو حركوا جسمًا ينبغي أن تحصل حركة كل جزء متبعضة على عدد الفاعلين، فتكون كل حركة منقسمة إلى الأعشار والأسداس وأكثر، وهذا جهل فاحش. وحكى ابن الراوندي عنه أنه كان يقول: التحريك هو عين الحركة، فلما كان التحريك والحركة واحدًا والدافعان محركان فالواحد يكون محركا، وإنما يكون محركًا أن لو وجد منه تحريك، والتحريك هو عين الحركة فيجب أن يحصل بكل واحد منهما حركة على حدة وهذا خلاف مذهبه وهذا كله هذيان، وتضييع الوقت بإبطاله عبث، إذ معرفة بطلان القول بتجزؤ الأعراض حاصلة بالطبائع، والله الموفق. وزعم هشام بن عمرو أن كل جزء من أجزاء الحجر يحصل فيه حركتان أحداهما فعل لهذا والأخرى فعل لذلك، وهذا محال، لأن الجزاء إن كان يخرج بأحديهما عن المكان الأول فالأحرى إذا لم تخرج بها عن المكان الأول فلا تكون حركة، وإن كان لا يخرج بأحديهما عن المكان الأول فهى ليست بحركة. فإن قال: يخرج بأحديهما إذا انفردت وبهما إذا اجتمعت. قلنا: إذا خرج الجزء بهما عن المكان الأول أخرج بهما جميعًا أم بأحديهما أم بكل واحد منهما؟ فإن قال: خرج بهما جميعًا فهما جميعًا علة واحدة فكانت كل واحدة منهما بعض العلة وهو باطل، لأنها لم تكن حركته، وإن قال: خرج بأحديهما. فالأخرى إذا لم تخرج بها عن المكان الأول فلم تكن حركة، وإن قال: خرج بكل واحد منهما. قلنا: فإذا خرج بما لولا هو لما انعدم الخروج، والخروج بمثل هذا عين معقول، إذ العلل العقلية موجبات بذواتها فما يتصور ثبوت معلول بدونه، ولم
The unity of the agent, and when it is not so when the agent is one, it indicates that it is not so when the agents are multiple. Then it is said to him: Does the body move away with half the motion that one of them produced? If he says: Yes. Then if he moved it, there would occur from each of the two movers a separate motion, and each mover would obtain a separate motion, and this is not his doctrine. If he says: Every half is not a motion, then if every half is not a removal, the two halves when they combine, then there has combined what is not a removal and what is not a removal, and by the combination of what is not a removal and what is not a removal and what is not a motion, and what is not a motion, the body does not leave the first place; and if it leaves, that indicates the existence of removal. Then it is said to him: If one of them produced in every part of the body's parts half the motion that is his share, does the body leave its first place? If he says: Yes, then it is a motion as we have shown. If he says: No, then it remains in the first place and remains stationary in it, so there would occur in it the combination of rest and some motion, and it is impossible for a thing to combine with some of its opposite; then the other half is also not a motion, so it is permissible for rest to combine with it as well, since rest may coincide with what is not a motion and what is not a motion, just as it coincides with color and taste. All this is ignorance and departure from knowledge. And the consequence of this is that if a large group moved a body, there would occur a motion of each part divided according to the number of agents, so each motion would be divided into tenths, sixths, and more, and this is gross ignorance. And Ibn al-Rawandi narrated from him that he used to say: The causing of motion is identical to motion itself. Since causing of motion and motion are one, and the two impellers are movers, so the one would be a mover, and it is only a mover if there occurred from him causing of motion, and causing of motion is identical to motion, so it is necessary that there occur from each of them a separate motion, and this contradicts his doctrine. All this is delirium, and wasting time in refuting it is frivolous, for knowledge of the falsehood of the claim of the divisibility of accidents is obtained by natural disposition. And God is the Grantor of success. And Hisham ibn Amr claimed that in every part of the stone's parts there occur two motions, one being the act of this [agent] and the other the act of that [agent]. This is impossible, because if the part leaves the first place by one of them, then it is more fitting [that it leaves]; if it does not leave by it, then it is not a motion. And if it does not leave the first place by one of them, then it is not a motion. If he says: It leaves by one of them when it is alone, and by both when they combine. We say: If the part leaves the first place by both, does it leave by both together, or by one of them, or by each one of them? If he says: It leaves by both together, then both together are one cause, so each one of them is part of the cause, and this is false, because it was not its motion. If he says: It leaves by one of them, then the other, since it does not cause it to leave the first place, was not a motion. If he says: It leaves by each one of them. We say: Then it leaves by that without which the leaving would not cease to exist, and leaving by such is rationally obvious, for rational causes necessitate by their essences, so it is inconceivable for an effect to subsist without it, and it has not...
Source pages p. 913

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the doctrine of the divisibility of accidents (tajazzu' al-a'rad), specifically concerning motion. It consists of two main parts: first, a logical argument showing the absurd consequences of holding that motion is composed of parts, and second, a critique of specific views held by Ibn al-Rawandi and Hisham ibn Amr.

The argument begins by considering the case of a single agent (al-fāʿil) moving a body. If motion were composed of parts, then when two agents each cause half the motion, absurdities follow. The author poses a dilemma: does the body move (zāl) with half the motion produced by one agent? If yes, then each agent produces a separate complete motion, contradicting the supposed division. If no, then half a motion is not motion at all, and combining two non-motions cannot produce motion. Furthermore, if each part of the body receives only half a motion, the body either moves or remains stationary. If it remains, that implies the impossible combination of rest and motion. The absurd consequence is that if many agents moved a body, the resulting motions would be fragmented into countless fractions, which is patent ignorance.

Then the text reports Ibn al-Rawandi's claim that causing motion (taḥrīk) is identical to motion (ḥaraka). If so, each mover would produce a separate motion, contradicting the original claim. The author dismisses this as delirium (hadhyān) and a waste of time, asserting that the falsity of the divisibility of accidents is known by natural disposition (bi-ṭ-ṭabāʾiʿ).

Finally, Hisham ibn Amr's view is refuted: he held that each part of a stone receives two motions from two agents. The author argues that if the part leaves its place by one motion, that one is sufficient; if not, then it is not a motion. When Hisham claims that it leaves by one when alone and by both when together, the author again poses a dilemma: does the part leave by both together (making them a single cause), by one alone (making the other superfluous), or by each independently (leading to the impossibility of two complete motions in one part). The argument concludes that rational causes necessitate their effects, so a single effect cannot have two independent causes. The passage ends abruptly, but the refutation is clear.

Key Terms
تجزؤ الأعراض
الحركة
الزوال
السكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Half a motion is still a motion because it causes movement.
Correction: The author argues that if half a motion caused movement, it would be a full motion; if not, it is not motion. A half-motion is not a motion.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the absurd consequence if many agents move a body under the theory of divisible motion?
A) The body would not move at all.
B) Each motion would be divided into tenths, sixths, and more.
C) The agents would cancel each other's motions.
D) The body would move in opposite directions.

Key Takeaway
Motion cannot be composed of parts; any attempt to divide it leads to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If motion cannot be divided among multiple agents, what does that mean for your daily assumption that your own efforts produce results? Are you truly the agent of even a single movement in your life, or is every motion directly from God?
?
The passage exposes the absurdity of thinking that parts of motion can add up. Consider your spiritual life: do you sometimes treat your good deeds as 'parts' of righteousness that you accumulate, forgetting that each deed is a complete gift from Allah?
?
The opponent's error is thinking half a motion is still a motion. In your reliance on God, are you tempted to believe that minimal obedience earns partial reward, or do you recognize that even the smallest act is either accepted as a whole by Allah or not at all? How will you change your perspective on worship today?

Refutation of Ibn al-Rawandi: The Identity of Tahrik and Haraka
Nasafi refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's identity thesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's critique of Ibn al-Rawandi's claim that causing motion is identical to motion itself.

Core Idea
If causing motion is identical to motion, each mover would produce a separate motion.

Ibn al-Rawandi, a controversial figure, held that the act of causing motion (tahrik) is the same as motion (haraka). Nasafi shows that this leads to the same absurdity of multiple motions from multiple agents, contradicting the original doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the short Arabic passage on Ibn al-Rawandi and its translation.
2
Understand the claim
Ibn al-Rawandi claimed that tahrik (causing motion) is identical to haraka (motion).
3
Analyze the consequence
If they are identical, then each mover must produce motion, leading to multiple motions from multiple agents.
4
Note Nasafi's dismissal
Nasafi calls this view 'delirium' and says the truth is known by natural disposition.

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the doctrine of the divisibility of accidents (tajazzu' al-a'rad), specifically concerning motion. It consists of two main parts: first, a logical argument showing the absurd consequences of holding that motion is composed of parts, and second, a critique of specific views held by Ibn al-Rawandi and Hisham ibn Amr.

Then the text reports Ibn al-Rawandi's claim that causing motion (taḥrīk) is identical to motion (ḥaraka). If so, each mover would produce a separate motion, contradicting the original claim. ?

?

Refutation of Hisham ibn Amr: Two Motions in One Part
Nasafi refutes Hisham's dual-motion theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's logical refutation of Hisham ibn Amr's claim that each part of a stone receives two motions from two agents.

Core Idea
Two motions in one part cannot both be causes of motion without contradiction.

Hisham ibn Amr argued that when two agents move a stone, each part of the stone receives two motions, one from each agent. Nasafi subjects this to rigorous logical examination, showing that it leads to impossible conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage on Hisham ibn Amr and its translation.
2
Understand Hisham's claim
Each part of a stone has two motions, one from each agent.
3
Follow the first dilemma
If one motion causes departure, the other is superfluous; if not, it's not motion.
4
Examine Hisham's reply
He says the part leaves by one alone and by both together.
5
Analyze the second dilemma
Nasafi asks: does the part leave by both together, one alone, or each independently? Each leads to absurdity.

متبعضة على عدد الفاعلين، فتكون كل حركة منقسمة إلى الأعشار والأسداس وأكثر، وهذا جهل فاحش. وحكى ابن الراوندي عنه أنه كان يقول: التحريك هو عين الحركة، فلما كان التحريك والحركة واحدًا والدافعان محركان فالواحد يكون محركا، وإنما يكون محركًا أن لو وجد منه تحريك، والتحريك هو عين الحركة فيجب أن يحصل بكل واحد منهما حركة على حدة وهذا خلاف مذهبه وهذا كله هذيان، وتضييع الوقت بإبطاله عبث، إذ معرفة بطلان القول بتجزؤ الأعراض حاصلة بالطبائع، والله الموفق. وزعم هشام بن عمرو أن كل جزء من أجزاء الحجر يحصل فيه حركتان أحداهما فعل لهذا والأخرى فعل لذلك، وهذا محال، لأن الجزاء إن كان يخرج بأحديهما عن المكان الأول فالأحرى إذا لم تخرج بها عن المكان الأول فلا تكون حركة، وإن كان لا يخرج بأحديهما عن المكان الأول فهى ليست بحركة. فإن قال: يخرج بأحديهما إذا انفردت وبهما إذا اجتمعت. قلنا: إذا خرج الجزء بهما عن المكان الأول أخرج بهما جميعًا أم بأحديهما أم بكل واحد منهما؟ فإن قال: خرج بهما جميعًا فهما جميعًا علة واحدة فكانت كل واحدة منهما بعض العلة وهو باطل، لأنها لم تكن حركته، وإن قال: خرج بأحديهما. فالأخرى إذا لم تخرج بها عن المكان الأول فلم تكن حركة، وإن قال: خرج بكل واحد منهما. قلنا: فإذا خرج بما لولا هو لما انعدم الخروج، والخروج بمثل هذا عين معقول، إذ العلل العقلية موجبات بذواتها فما يتصور ثبوت معلول بدونه، ولم
The unity of the agent, and when it is not so when the agent is one, it indicates that it is not so when the agents are multiple. Then it is said to him: Does the body move away with half the motion that one of them produced? If he says: Yes. Then if he moved it, there would occur from each of the two movers a separate motion, and each mover would obtain a separate motion, and this is not his doctrine. If he says: Every half is not a motion, then if every half is not a removal, the two halves when they combine, then there has combined what is not a removal and what is not a removal, and by the combination of what is not a removal and what is not a removal and what is not a motion, and what is not a motion, the body does not leave the first place; and if it leaves, that indicates the existence of removal. Then it is said to him: If one of them produced in every part of the body's parts half the motion that is his share, does the body leave its first place? If he says: Yes, then it is a motion as we have shown. If he says: No, then it remains in the first place and remains stationary in it, so there would occur in it the combination of rest and some motion, and it is impossible for a thing to combine with some of its opposite; then the other half is also not a motion, so it is permissible for rest to combine with it as well, since rest may coincide with what is not a motion and what is not a motion, just as it coincides with color and taste. All this is ignorance and departure from knowledge. And the consequence of this is that if a large group moved a body, there would occur a motion of each part divided according to the number of agents, so each motion would be divided into tenths, sixths, and more, and this is gross ignorance. And Ibn al-Rawandi narrated from him that he used to say: The causing of motion is identical to motion itself. Since causing of motion and motion are one, and the two impellers are movers, so the one would be a mover, and it is only a mover if there occurred from him causing of motion, and causing of motion is identical to motion, so it is necessary that there occur from each of them a separate motion, and this contradicts his doctrine. All this is delirium, and wasting time in refuting it is frivolous, for knowledge of the falsehood of the claim of the divisibility of accidents is obtained by natural disposition. And God is the Grantor of success. And Hisham ibn Amr claimed that in every part of the stone's parts there occur two motions, one being the act of this [agent] and the other the act of that [agent]. This is impossible, because if the part leaves the first place by one of them, then it is more fitting [that it leaves]; if it does not leave by it, then it is not a motion. And if it does not leave the first place by one of them, then it is not a motion. If he says: It leaves by one of them when it is alone, and by both when they combine. We say: If the part leaves the first place by both, does it leave by both together, or by one of them, or by each one of them? If he says: It leaves by both together, then both together are one cause, so each one of them is part of the cause, and this is false, because it was not its motion. If he says: It leaves by one of them, then the other, since it does not cause it to leave the first place, was not a motion. If he says: It leaves by each one of them. We say: Then it leaves by that without which the leaving would not cease to exist, and leaving by such is rationally obvious, for rational causes necessitate by their essences, so it is inconceivable for an effect to subsist without it, and it has not...
Source pages p. 913

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the doctrine of the divisibility of accidents (tajazzu' al-a'rad), specifically concerning motion. It consists of two main parts: first, a logical argument showing the absurd consequences of holding that motion is composed of parts, and second, a critique of specific views held by Ibn al-Rawandi and Hisham ibn Amr.

The argument begins by considering the case of a single agent (al-fāʿil) moving a body. If motion were composed of parts, then when two agents each cause half the motion, absurdities follow. The author poses a dilemma: does the body move (zāl) with half the motion produced by one agent? If yes, then each agent produces a separate complete motion, contradicting the supposed division. If no, then half a motion is not motion at all, and combining two non-motions cannot produce motion. Furthermore, if each part of the body receives only half a motion, the body either moves or remains stationary. If it remains, that implies the impossible combination of rest and motion. The absurd consequence is that if many agents moved a body, the resulting motions would be fragmented into countless fractions, which is patent ignorance.

Then the text reports Ibn al-Rawandi's claim that causing motion (taḥrīk) is identical to motion (ḥaraka). If so, each mover would produce a separate motion, contradicting the original claim. The author dismisses this as delirium (hadhyān) and a waste of time, asserting that the falsity of the divisibility of accidents is known by natural disposition (bi-ṭ-ṭabāʾiʿ).

Finally, Hisham ibn Amr's view is refuted: he held that each part of a stone receives two motions from two agents. The author argues that if the part leaves its place by one motion, that one is sufficient; if not, then it is not a motion. When Hisham claims that it leaves by one when alone and by both when together, the author again poses a dilemma: does the part leave by both together (making them a single cause), by one alone (making the other superfluous), or by each independently (leading to the impossibility of two complete motions in one part). The argument concludes that rational causes necessitate their effects, so a single effect cannot have two independent causes. The passage ends abruptly, but the refutation is clear.

Key Terms
هشام بن عمرو
العلة
الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two independent causes can produce a single effect (motion of a part).
Correction: Nasafi argues that a single effect cannot have two independent causes; either they act as one cause or one is redundant.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi argue if Hisham says the part leaves by both together?
A) Both together are one cause, so each is a part cause, which is false.
B) Both are separate motions.
C) It is possible.
D) The part does not move.

Key Takeaway
A single effect (motion of a part) cannot be caused independently by two separate motions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed a good outcome in your life to multiple 'causes'—your effort, someone's help, luck—while neglecting that Allah alone is the true cause? This lesson dismantles the illusion of multiple independent causes; will you now audit your heart for any false partners you assign to Allah's sole causality?
?
When you rely on a created means (like a doctor, money, or skill), do you see it as a mere conduit or as an independent cause? The refutation of Hisham shows that if a means does not produce the effect by itself, it is not a cause at all. How will you shift your reliance from means to the Originator?
?
The impossibility of two independent causes for one effect is a logical truth that reflects tawhid. What area of your life do you still mentally divide—thinking both Allah and your own planning determine success? What step will you take today to entrust that area entirely to Allah, affirming 'There is no cause but Allah'?

Refutation of Ibn al-Rawandi's Identity Claim
Reports and refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's identity of causing motion and motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn al-Rawandi's claim that causing motion is identical to motion leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
If causing motion equals motion, then two movers imply two motions, contradicting one atom.

The author begins by reporting a view from Ibn al-Rawandi that equates causing motion with motion itself. This seemingly simple claim has a devastating logical flaw when applied to two movers acting on one atom. We will see how the author exposes the contradiction and dismisses the position as nonsense.

Guided Path
1
Read the report of Ibn al-Rawandi's view
Carefully read the Arabic passage where Ibn al-Rawandi's claim is stated: that causing motion (taḥrīk) is identical to motion (ḥaraka). Notice the compressed reasoning.
2
Follow the reductio
The author shows that if two movers cause motion, and causing motion is motion itself, then each mover must produce a separate motion. But an atom cannot have two distinct motions. Identify the absurd conclusion.
3
Understand why this is dismissible
The author labels this 'hudhayān' (nonsense). Recognize that the identity claim is self-defeating because it forces multiple motions where only one is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكى ابن الراوندي عنه أنه كان يقول: التحريك هو عين الحركة، فلما كان التحريك والحركة واحدًا والدافعان محركان فالواحد يكون محركا، وإنما يكون محركًا أن لو وجد منه تحريك، والتحريك هو عين الحركة فيجب أن يحصل بكل واحد منهما حركة على حدة وهذا خلاف مذهبه وهذا كله هذيان. وزعم هشام بن عمرو أن كل جزء من أجزاء الحجر يحصل فيه حركتان أحداهما فعل لهذا والأخرى فعل لذلك، وهذا محال، لأن الجزء إن كان يخرج بأحديهما عن المكان الأول فالأحرى إذا لم تخرج بها عن المكان الأول فلا تكون حركة، وإن كان لا يخرج بأحديهما عن المكان الأول فهى ليست بحركة. فإن قال: يخرج بأحديهما إذا انفردت وبهما إذا اجتمعت. قلنا: إذا خرج الجزء بهما عن المكان الأول أخرج بهما جميعًا أم بأحديهما أم بكل واحد منهما؟ فإن قال: خرج بهما جميعًا فهما جميعًا علة واحدة فكانت كل واحدة منهما بعض العلة وهو باطل، لأنها لم تكن حركته، وإن قال: خرج بأحديهما فالأخرى إذا لم تخرج بها عن المكان الأول فلم تكن حركة، وإن قال: خرج بكل واحد منهما. قلنا: فإذا خرج بما لولا هو لما انعدم الخروج، والخروج بمثل هذا عين معقول، إذ العلل العقلية موجبات بذواتها فما يتصور ثبوت معلول بدونه، ولم ينعدم المعلول لو انعدم هو لا يكون علة بالثبوت، ولو كان لما كان به فكانت إحداهما غير موجبة للخروج فلم تكن حركة. يحققه: أن هذا الجزء ما قطع إلا مكانا وحدا، ولو جاز لك أن تقول: وجدت حركتان لوجود محركين كان لغيرك أن يقول: لا بل وجدت حركة لاتحاد المكان الذي قطع ويقال له: لو جاز وجود حركتين لا يقطع بهما إلا مكان واحد لجاز وجود ألف حركة لا يقطع بهما إلا مكان واحد، وهو يلتزم هنا. ويقول: لو حرك الجسم ألف نفر وأكثر حدث في كل جزء من أجزائه من الحركات بعدد الفاعلين، والقول بوجود ألف حركة لا يقطع بها إلا مكان واحد تجاهل، ولو جاز أن يخطر أحد هذا بباله لجاز لغيره أن يقول على القلب، ويجوز أن يقطع ألف مكان بحركة واحدة. وهذا كله هذيان، فاقتضت بهم أصولهم الفاسدة إلى أن جوزوا تارة قيام عرض واحد بألف محل وأكثر، وجوزوا تارة قيام ألف عرض من جنس واحد وأكثر بمحل لا أثر لها إلا ما لواحد منها، وهذا كله محال. ومن دأب المعتزلة عدم المبالاة بالانسلاخ عن الدين، وارتكاب ما يأباه العقل وينادي بإحالته ويحكم على قائله بالتجاهل والخروج عن المعارف عند رجائهم نصرة ما هم عليه من الباطل، ودفع ما يتوجه عليهم من الحجج الهادفة لقواعد ما هم عليه من الضلال الآتية على ما يتمسكون به من البدع بالإبطال والاستئصال والرجوع إلى الطريق المستبين والصراط المستقيم خير من الإمعان في المهاوي والمهالك والإيجاف في البوادي والمجاهل، ونعوذ بالله
And Ibn al-Rawandi reported from him that he used to say: 'Causing motion (al-taḥrīk) is identical to motion (al-ḥaraka).' Since causing motion and motion are one, and the two pushers are movers, then the one should be a mover. But it would only be a mover if causing motion occurs from it, and causing motion is identical to motion, so it is necessary that from each one a separate motion occurs, and this contradicts his doctrine. All of this is nonsense. And Hisham ibn Amr claimed that in every part of a stone two motions occur, one being the act of this [mover] and the other an act of that [mover]. This is impossible, because if the part exits the first place by one of them, then it is more appropriate [to consider it motion], but if it does not exit by it from the first place then it is not motion. And if it does not exit by one of them from the first place, then it is not motion. If he says: 'It exits by one of them when isolated, and by both when combined.' We say: 'When the part exits the first place by both, does it exit by both together, or by one of them, or by each one separately?' If he says: 'It exits by both together,' then both together are one cause, so each one of them is part of the cause, which is false, because it would not be its motion. If he says: 'It exits by one of them,' then the other, since it does not cause exit from the first place, is not motion. If he says: 'It exits by each one separately.' We say: 'If it exits by that without which the exit would not cease to be, then exit by such [a thing] is rationally inconceivable, for intelligible causes are necessitating by their essences, so it is impossible to conceive the existence of an effect without it. And if the effect would not cease to exist if it ceased to be, then it is not a cause in terms of affirmation. And if it were, then what would be through it? So one of them would not be necessitating the exit, and thus it would not be motion.' This is confirmed by: This part has only traversed a single space. And if it were permissible for you to say: 'Two motions occurred due to the existence of two movers,' then someone else could say: 'No, rather one motion occurred due to the unity of the space traversed.' And it would be said to him: 'If it were permissible for two motions to exist by which only one space is traversed, then it would be permissible for a thousand motions to exist by which only one space is traversed.' And he would accept that here. And he says: 'If a thousand or more persons move a body, there would occur in each part of its parts motions equal to the number of agents.' The statement that a thousand motions exist by which only one space is traversed is foolishness. And if it were permissible for anyone to think this, it would be permissible for another to say the opposite, and it would be permissible for a thousand spaces to be traversed by a single motion. All of this is nonsense. Their corrupt principles led them to sometimes allow one accident to inhere in a thousand or more loci, and sometimes to allow a thousand accidents of the same kind or more to inhere in a locus with no effect except what a single one would have. All of this is impossible. And the habit of the Mu'tazila is disregard for slipping away from the religion and committing what reason rejects and declares impossible and judges its proponent to be foolish and departure from rational knowledge when they hope to support the falsehood they are upon and to repel the arguments directed against the foundations of their misguidance that come to invalidate and uproot the innovations they cling to. And returning to the clear path and the straight way is better than persisting in precipices and perils and stumbling in wastelands and ignorances. And we seek refuge in Allah.
Source pages p. 914

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claims that a single atom can have multiple motions simultaneously. The author first reports Ibn al-Rawandi's view that causing motion is identical to motion, then shows its absurd consequence: if two movers cause motion in one thing, since causing motion is identical to motion, each would produce a separate motion, yet the atom cannot have two distinct motions. This is dismissed as nonsense. Then the author discusses Hisham ibn Amr's claim that each part of a stone receives two motions, one from each mover. The author argues: if the part leaves its place by one of them, then the other does not cause leaving, so it is not motion. If neither causes leaving, then neither is motion. The objector may say that each motion alone would cause leaving, but together they also cause leaving. The author then enters a dialectic: if the part leaves by both together, then both together are one cause, so each is only part of the cause, but partial causes do not cause motion individually. If it leaves by one alone, then the other is not a cause. If it leaves by each separately, then the exit is caused by each independently, which is impossible because an effect cannot be produced without its cause. In rational causality, causes necessitate their effects essentially, so an effect cannot occur without its cause, but here the exit occurs even if one motion is absent, making it not a cause. Therefore, one of the two motions is not necessitating the exit, so it is not a motion. The passage concludes with a reductio ad absurdum: the atom only traverses one space; if two motions could traverse the same space, then a thousand motions could also traverse one space, which is absurd. The Mu'tazilites would accept this, claiming that a thousand movers produce a thousand motions in one atom. This is dismissed as ignorant. The author then generalizes: their corrupt principles allow one accident in many loci or many accidents of one kind in one locus with the effect of one. All this is impossible. Finally, the author condemns the Mu'tazilites for their disregard of reason and religion, urging return to the straight path.

Key Terms
تحريك (taḥrīk)
حركة (ḥaraka)
محرك (muḥarrik)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That causing motion is the same as the motion itself.
Correction: This leads to the absurdity that two movers produce two motions in one atom, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of Ibn al-Rawandi's claim that causing motion is identical to motion?
A) Two movers would produce one motion together.
B) Each mover would produce a separate motion, leading to multiple motions in one atom.
C) Motion would be impossible.
D) The atom would be destroyed.

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Rawandi's equation of causing motion with motion is self-refuting because it entails multiple motions in a single atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe your actions are your own, but do you ever confuse the 'causing' of an action with the action itself, so that you miss the deeper accountability? If causing and being are the same in your mind, how would you separate intention from deed in front of Allah?
?
Ibn al-Rawandi's claim collapses because it forces two motions in one atom. Where in your life have you tried to reconcile two opposing desires as if they were one, only to find that your heart cannot hold both? Will you let this lesson push you to choose?
?
The passage dismisses the whole idea as nonsense. When you read about a belief that contradicts the truth, do you feel the urgency to reject it, or do you let it sit in your mind as a harmless intellectual game? This is a matter of Allah's creation—is it a game?

Refutation of Hisham ibn Amr's Two Motions
Presents and refutes Hisham ibn Amr's claim of two motions per atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic argument against Hisham ibn Amr's claim that each atom contains two motions from two movers.

Core Idea
If one motion causes the atom to leave its place, the other is redundant; if not, it is not motion.

Hisham ibn Amr boldly claimed that each part of a stone receives two motions, one from each mover. The author immediately presents a simple dilemma: either each motion causes the atom to leave its place or it does not. This dilemma exposes the impossibility of two motions in one atom.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read Hisham's claim that every part of a stone has two motions, each from a different mover.
2
Follow the dilemma
The author asks: does each motion cause the part to exit its place? If one does, the other is unnecessary. If neither does, neither is motion.
3
Appreciate the force
This simple logical check shows that two motions cannot both be genuine motions affecting the same atom.

Source Text

يحصل بكل واحد منهما حركة على حدة وهذا خلاف مذهبه وهذا كله هذيان. وزعم هشام بن عمرو أن كل جزء من أجزاء الحجر يحصل فيه حركتان أحداهما فعل لهذا والأخرى فعل لذلك، وهذا محال، لأن الجزء إن كان يخرج بأحديهما عن المكان الأول فالأحرى إذا لم تخرج بها عن المكان الأول فلا تكون حركة، وإن كان لا يخرج بأحديهما عن المكان الأول فهى ليست بحركة. فإن قال: يخرج بأحديهما إذا انفردت وبهما إذا اجتمعت. قلنا: إذا خرج الجزء بهما عن المكان الأول أخرج بهما جميعًا أم بأحديهما أم بكل واحد منهما؟ فإن قال: خرج بهما جميعًا فهما جميعًا علة واحدة فكانت كل واحدة منهما بعض العلة وهو باطل، لأنها لم تكن حركته، وإن قال: خرج بأحديهما فالأخرى إذا لم تخرج بها عن المكان الأول فلم تكن حركة، وإن قال: خرج بكل واحد منهما. قلنا: فإذا خرج بما لولا هو لما انعدم الخروج، والخروج بمثل هذا عين معقول، إذ العلل العقلية موجبات بذواتها فما يتصور ثبوت معلول بدونه، ولم ينعدم المعلول لو انعدم هو لا يكون علة بالثبوت، ولو كان لما كان به فكانت إحداهما غير موجبة للخروج فلم تكن حركة. يحققه: أن هذا الجزء ما قطع إلا مكانا وحدا، ولو جاز لك أن تقول: وجدت حركتان لوجود محركين كان لغيرك أن يقول: لا بل وجدت حركة لاتحاد المكان الذي قطع ويقال له: لو جاز وجود حركتين لا يقطع بهما إلا مكان واحد لجاز وجود ألف حركة لا يقطع بهما إلا مكان واحد، وهو يلتزم هنا. ويقول: لو حرك الجسم ألف نفر وأكثر حدث في كل جزء من أجزائه من الحركات بعدد الفاعلين، والقول بوجود ألف حركة لا يقطع بها إلا مكان واحد تجاهل، ولو جاز أن يخطر أحد هذا بباله لجاز لغيره أن يقول على القلب، ويجوز أن يقطع ألف مكان بحركة واحدة. وهذا كله هذيان، فاقتضت بهم أصولهم الفاسدة إلى أن جوزوا تارة قيام عرض واحد بألف محل وأكثر، وجوزوا تارة قيام ألف عرض من جنس واحد وأكثر بمحل لا أثر لها إلا ما لواحد منها، وهذا كله محال. ومن دأب المعتزلة عدم المبالاة بالانسلاخ عن الدين، وارتكاب ما يأباه العقل وينادي بإحالته ويحكم على قائله بالتجاهل والخروج عن المعارف عند رجائهم نصرة ما هم عليه من الباطل، ودفع ما يتوجه عليهم من الحجج الهادفة لقواعد ما هم عليه من الضلال الآتية على ما يتمسكون به من البدع بالإبطال والاستئصال والرجوع إلى الطريق المستبين والصراط المستقيم خير من الإمعان في المهاوي والمهالك والإيجاف في البوادي والمجاهل، ونعوذ بالله
And Ibn al-Rawandi reported from him that he used to say: 'Causing motion (al-taḥrīk) is identical to motion (al-ḥaraka).' Since causing motion and motion are one, and the two pushers are movers, then the one should be a mover. But it would only be a mover if causing motion occurs from it, and causing motion is identical to motion, so it is necessary that from each one a separate motion occurs, and this contradicts his doctrine. All of this is nonsense. And Hisham ibn Amr claimed that in every part of a stone two motions occur, one being the act of this [mover] and the other an act of that [mover]. This is impossible, because if the part exits the first place by one of them, then it is more appropriate [to consider it motion], but if it does not exit by it from the first place then it is not motion. And if it does not exit by one of them from the first place, then it is not motion. If he says: 'It exits by one of them when isolated, and by both when combined.' We say: 'When the part exits the first place by both, does it exit by both together, or by one of them, or by each one separately?' If he says: 'It exits by both together,' then both together are one cause, so each one of them is part of the cause, which is false, because it would not be its motion. If he says: 'It exits by one of them,' then the other, since it does not cause exit from the first place, is not motion. If he says: 'It exits by each one separately.' We say: 'If it exits by that without which the exit would not cease to be, then exit by such [a thing] is rationally inconceivable, for intelligible causes are necessitating by their essences, so it is impossible to conceive the existence of an effect without it. And if the effect would not cease to exist if it ceased to be, then it is not a cause in terms of affirmation. And if it were, then what would be through it? So one of them would not be necessitating the exit, and thus it would not be motion.' This is confirmed by: This part has only traversed a single space. And if it were permissible for you to say: 'Two motions occurred due to the existence of two movers,' then someone else could say: 'No, rather one motion occurred due to the unity of the space traversed.' And it would be said to him: 'If it were permissible for two motions to exist by which only one space is traversed, then it would be permissible for a thousand motions to exist by which only one space is traversed.' And he would accept that here. And he says: 'If a thousand or more persons move a body, there would occur in each part of its parts motions equal to the number of agents.' The statement that a thousand motions exist by which only one space is traversed is foolishness. And if it were permissible for anyone to think this, it would be permissible for another to say the opposite, and it would be permissible for a thousand spaces to be traversed by a single motion. All of this is nonsense. Their corrupt principles led them to sometimes allow one accident to inhere in a thousand or more loci, and sometimes to allow a thousand accidents of the same kind or more to inhere in a locus with no effect except what a single one would have. All of this is impossible. And the habit of the Mu'tazila is disregard for slipping away from the religion and committing what reason rejects and declares impossible and judges its proponent to be foolish and departure from rational knowledge when they hope to support the falsehood they are upon and to repel the arguments directed against the foundations of their misguidance that come to invalidate and uproot the innovations they cling to. And returning to the clear path and the straight way is better than persisting in precipices and perils and stumbling in wastelands and ignorances. And we seek refuge in Allah.
Source pages p. 914

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claims that a single atom can have multiple motions simultaneously. The author first reports Ibn al-Rawandi's view that causing motion is identical to motion, then shows its absurd consequence: if two movers cause motion in one thing, since causing motion is identical to motion, each would produce a separate motion, yet the atom cannot have two distinct motions. This is dismissed as nonsense. Then the author discusses Hisham ibn Amr's claim that each part of a stone receives two motions, one from each mover. The author argues: if the part leaves its place by one of them, then the other does not cause leaving, so it is not motion. If neither causes leaving, then neither is motion. The objector may say that each motion alone would cause leaving, but together they also cause leaving. The author then enters a dialectic: if the part leaves by both together, then both together are one cause, so each is only part of the cause, but partial causes do not cause motion individually. If it leaves by one alone, then the other is not a cause. If it leaves by each separately, then the exit is caused by each independently, which is impossible because an effect cannot be produced without its cause. In rational causality, causes necessitate their effects essentially, so an effect cannot occur without its cause, but here the exit occurs even if one motion is absent, making it not a cause. Therefore, one of the two motions is not necessitating the exit, so it is not a motion. The passage concludes with a reductio ad absurdum: the atom only traverses one space; if two motions could traverse the same space, then a thousand motions could also traverse one space, which is absurd. The Mu'tazilites would accept this, claiming that a thousand movers produce a thousand motions in one atom. This is dismissed as ignorant. The author then generalizes: their corrupt principles allow one accident in many loci or many accidents of one kind in one locus with the effect of one. All this is impossible. Finally, the author condemns the Mu'tazilites for their disregard of reason and religion, urging return to the straight path.

Key Terms
جزء (juzʾ)
حركة (ḥaraka)
مكان (makān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That two motions can coexist in one atom as separate acts.
Correction: Only one motion can cause displacement; the other is either redundant or not motion.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main objection to Hisham ibn Amr's claim?
A) Two motions would require two atoms.
B) If one motion causes the atom to leave its place, the other motion is unnecessary and not a real motion.
C) Motion cannot be divided.
D) The stone would break.

Key Takeaway
Hisham's claim fails because only one motion can cause spatial displacement in an atom; any second motion is either superfluous or non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held two contradictory beliefs about the same matter, assuming both could be true without checking? How does this lesson's logical scrutiny challenge you to examine your own convictions for hidden contradictions? What specific step will you take this week to resolve one inconsistency in your understanding of faith?
?
When you face a theological dilemma, do you tend to settle for ambiguity or pursue a precise resolution? What does this lesson teach you about the importance of clear definitions and logical consistency in your belief about God's actions? Commit to one area where you will stop accepting vague explanations and seek clarity.
?
This lesson shows that two conflicting claims about a single atom lead to absurdity. In your own life, what 'atoms'—core truths or commitments—are you trying to assign conflicting causes or explanations? Confront one such tension today and decide which explanation is truly motion (living) and which is not, based on God's revelation and sound reason.

Causality and Created Things

Dialectic on Combined vs Separate Exit
Dialectical refutation of combined or separate causation of exit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sophisticated dialectical argument that refutes the idea of two motions causing exit together or separately.

Core Idea
Two motions cannot be separate causes of one exit; that violates causality.

The opponent might argue that the atom exits by both motions together, or by each separately. The author systematically dismantles these possibilities using the principle of causality. This lesson demonstrates how the author applies rigorous reasoning to show that two motions cannot both be causes of a single displacement.

Guided Path
1
Consider the opponent's counter
The opponent claims that the atom exits by one motion when alone, and by both when combined.
2
Explore the three options
The author asks: does the exit occur by both together, by one, or by each separately? Each option is analyzed.
3
Understand the causality principle
The key is that causes necessitate their effects essentially. If exit occurs even without one motion, that motion is not a cause.

Source Text

وحكى ابن الراوندي عنه أنه كان يقول: التحريك هو عين الحركة، فلما كان التحريك والحركة واحدًا والدافعان محركان فالواحد يكون محركا، وإنما يكون محركًا أن لو وجد منه تحريك، والتحريك هو عين الحركة فيجب أن يحصل بكل واحد منهما حركة على حدة وهذا خلاف مذهبه وهذا كله هذيان. وزعم هشام بن عمرو أن كل جزء من أجزاء الحجر يحصل فيه حركتان أحداهما فعل لهذا والأخرى فعل لذلك، وهذا محال، لأن الجزء إن كان يخرج بأحديهما عن المكان الأول فالأحرى إذا لم تخرج بها عن المكان الأول فلا تكون حركة، وإن كان لا يخرج بأحديهما عن المكان الأول فهى ليست بحركة. فإن قال: يخرج بأحديهما إذا انفردت وبهما إذا اجتمعت. قلنا: إذا خرج الجزء بهما عن المكان الأول أخرج بهما جميعًا أم بأحديهما أم بكل واحد منهما؟ فإن قال: خرج بهما جميعًا فهما جميعًا علة واحدة فكانت كل واحدة منهما بعض العلة وهو باطل، لأنها لم تكن حركته، وإن قال: خرج بأحديهما فالأخرى إذا لم تخرج بها عن المكان الأول فلم تكن حركة، وإن قال: خرج بكل واحد منهما. قلنا: فإذا خرج بما لولا هو لما انعدم الخروج، والخروج بمثل هذا عين معقول، إذ العلل العقلية موجبات بذواتها فما يتصور ثبوت معلول بدونه، ولم ينعدم المعلول لو انعدم هو لا يكون علة بالثبوت، ولو كان لما كان به فكانت إحداهما غير موجبة للخروج فلم تكن حركة. يحققه: أن هذا الجزء ما قطع إلا مكانا وحدا، ولو جاز لك أن تقول: وجدت ويقول: لو حرك الجسم ألف نفر وأكثر حدث في كل جزء من أجزائه من الحركات بعدد الفاعلين، والقول بوجود ألف حركة لا يقطع بها إلا مكان واحد تجاهل، ولو جاز أن يخطر أحد هذا بباله لجاز لغيره أن يقول على القلب، ويجوز أن يقطع ألف مكان بحركة واحدة. وهذا كله هذيان، فاقتضت بهم أصولهم الفاسدة إلى أن جوزوا تارة قيام عرض واحد بألف محل وأكثر، وجوزوا تارة قيام ألف عرض من جنس واحد وأكثر بمحل لا أثر لها إلا ما لواحد منها، وهذا كله محال. ومن دأب المعتزلة عدم المبالاة بالانسلاخ عن الدين، وارتكاب ما يأباه العقل وينادي بإحالته ويحكم على قائله بالتجاهل والخروج عن المعارف عند رجائهم نصرة ما هم عليه من الباطل، ودفع ما يتوجه عليهم من الحجج الهادفة لقواعد ما هم عليه من الضلال الآتية على ما يتمسكون به من البدع بالإبطال والاستئصال والرجوع إلى الطريق المستبين والصراط المستقيم خير من الإمعان في المهاوي والمهالك والإيجاف Commentary

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That two motions can each be full causes of the same exit.
Correction: If one motion suffices, the other is unnecessary; if both are needed, each is only partial, so individually not motion.

Checkpoint
What is the problem with the opponent saying 'it exits by each one separately'?
A) It would mean the atom exits twice.
B) It violates causality because the exit would occur even if one motion were absent.
C) It requires two spaces.
D) It implies the motions are identical.

Key Takeaway
Two motions cannot both be genuine causes of a single displacement; rational causality requires one effect per cause.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Reductio ad Absurdum: Thousand Motions
Reductio ad absurdum leading to thousand motions and final condemnation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio ad absurdum that if two motions in one atom are allowed, then a thousand motions must also be allowed, leading to absurdity.

Core Idea
Allowing two motions in one atom logically forces accepting a thousand motions.

The author now extends the argument with a powerful reductio. If the opponent allows two motions to traverse one place, then by the same logic a thousand motions could also traverse the same place. The Mu'tazilites absurdly accept this, leading to conclusions that defy reason. This lesson exposes the ultimate folly of the position.

Guided Path
1
Follow the reductio
The author argues that if two motions can occur in one atom, then there is no limit; a thousand motions could also occur. Read the reasoning carefully.
2
See the opponent's acceptance
The opponent actually accepts a thousand motions from a thousand movers, claiming each part gets as many motions as agents.
3
Appreciate the absurdity
The author calls this 'tajāhul' (foolishness) and argues it leads to even greater absurdities, like one motion covering a thousand places.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يحصل بكل واحد منهما حركة على حدة وهذا خلاف مذهبه وهذا كله هذيان. وزعم هشام بن عمرو أن كل جزء من أجزاء الحجر يحصل فيه حركتان أحداهما فعل لهذا والأخرى فعل لذلك، وهذا محال، لأن الجزء إن كان يخرج بأحديهما
