يتوجه عليهم من الحجج الهادفة لقواعد ما هم عليه من الضلال الآتية على ما يتمسكون به من البدع بالإبطال والاستئصال والرجوع إلى الطريق المستبين والصراط المستقيم خير من الإمعان في المهاوي والمهالك والإيجاف في البوادي والمجاهل، ونعوذ بالله
And Ibn al-Rawandi reported from him that he used to say: 'Causing motion (al-taḥrīk) is identical to motion (al-ḥaraka).' Since causing motion and motion are one, and the two pushers are movers, then the one should be a mover. But it would only be a mover if causing motion occurs from it, and causing motion is identical to motion, so it is necessary that from each one a separate motion occurs, and this contradicts his doctrine. All of this is nonsense. And Hisham ibn Amr claimed that in every part of a stone two motions occur, one being the act of this [mover] and the other an act of that [mover]. This is impossible, because if the part exits the first place by one of them, then it is more appropriate [to consider it motion], but if it does not exit by it from the first place then it is not motion. And if it does not exit by one of them from the first place, then it is not motion. If he says: 'It exits by one of them when isolated, and by both when combined.' We say: 'When the part exits the first place by both, does it exit by both together, or by one of them, or by each one separately?' If he says: 'It exits by both together,' then both together are one cause, so each one of them is part of the cause, which is false, because it would not be its motion. If he says: 'It exits by one of them,' then the other, since it does not cause exit from the first place, is not motion. If he says: 'It exits by each one separately.' We say: 'If it exits by that without which the exit would not cease to be, then exit by such [a thing] is rationally inconceivable, for intelligible causes are necessitating by their essences, so it is impossible to conceive the existence of an effect without it. And if the effect would not cease to exist if it ceased to be, then it is not a cause in terms of affirmation. And if it were, then what would be through it? So one of them would not be necessitating the exit, and thus it would not be motion.' This is confirmed by: This part has only traversed a single space. And if it were permissible for you to say: 'Two motions occurred due to the existence of two movers,' then someone else could say: 'No, rather one motion occurred due to the unity of the space traversed.' And it would be said to him: 'If it were permissible for two motions to exist by which only one space is traversed, then it would be permissible for a thousand motions to exist by which only one space is traversed.' And he would accept that here. And he says: 'If a thousand or more persons move a body, there would occur in each part of its parts motions equal to the number of agents.' The statement that a thousand motions exist by which only one space is traversed is foolishness. And if it were permissible for anyone to think this, it would be permissible for another to say the opposite, and it would be permissible for a thousand spaces to be traversed by a single motion. All of this is nonsense. Their corrupt principles led them to sometimes allow one accident to inhere in a thousand or more loci, and sometimes to allow a thousand accidents of the same kind or more to inhere in a locus with no effect except what a single one would have. All of this is impossible. And the habit of the Mu'tazila is disregard for slipping away from the religion and committing what reason rejects and declares impossible and judges its proponent to be foolish and departure from rational knowledge when they hope to support the falsehood they are upon and to repel the arguments directed against the foundations of their misguidance that come to invalidate and uproot the innovations they cling to. And returning to the clear path and the straight way is better than persisting in precipices and perils and stumbling in wastelands and ignorances. And we seek refuge in Allah.
Source pages p. 914

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claims that a single atom can have multiple motions simultaneously. The author first reports Ibn al-Rawandi's view that causing motion is identical to motion, then shows its absurd consequence: if two movers cause motion in one thing, since causing motion is identical to motion, each would produce a separate motion, yet the atom cannot have two distinct motions. This is dismissed as nonsense. Then the author discusses Hisham ibn Amr's claim that each part of a stone receives two motions, one from each mover. The author argues: if the part leaves its place by one of them, then the other does not cause leaving, so it is not motion. If neither causes leaving, then neither is motion. The objector may say that each motion alone would cause leaving, but together they also cause leaving. The author then enters a dialectic: if the part leaves by both together, then both together are one cause, so each is only part of the cause, but partial causes do not cause motion individually. If it leaves by one alone, then the other is not a cause. If it leaves by each separately, then the exit is caused by each independently, which is impossible because an effect cannot be produced without its cause. In rational causality, causes necessitate their effects essentially, so an effect cannot occur without its cause, but here the exit occurs even if one motion is absent, making it not a cause. Therefore, one of the two motions is not necessitating the exit, so it is not a motion. The passage concludes with a reductio ad absurdum: the atom only traverses one space; if two motions could traverse the same space, then a thousand motions could also traverse one space, which is absurd. The Mu'tazilites would accept this, claiming that a thousand movers produce a thousand motions in one atom. This is dismissed as ignorant. The author then generalizes: their corrupt principles allow one accident in many loci or many accidents of one kind in one locus with the effect of one. All this is impossible. Finally, the author condemns the Mu'tazilites for their disregard of reason and religion, urging return to the straight path.

Key Terms
مكان (makān)
حركة (ḥaraka)
انسلاخ عن الدين (insilākh ʿan al-dīn)
معتزلة (Muʿtazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That two motions in one atom is acceptable without leading to further absurdities.
Correction: It logically forces accepting a thousand motions in one atom, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What does the reductio ad absurdum show about the claim that two motions can traverse one place?
A) It is impossible because motion is continuous.
B) If two are allowed, then a thousand are also allowed, leading to absurdity.
C) Two motions can never coexist in time.
D) The atom would be destroyed.

Key Takeaway
Allowing multiple motions in one atom leads to an infinite regress and absurd conclusions, exposing the irrationality of the Mu'tazilite position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself defending a belief that your intellect subtly warns you against, do you push through the discomfort out of pride or fear of being wrong? This passage calls that 'slipping away from religion.' Where in your life are you rationalizing a position you know is weak?
?
The author says 'returning to the clear path is better than persisting in precipices and perils.' What is one stubborn conviction you cling to—about yourself, others, or your deen—that deep down you suspect is a precipice? Will you turn back today, or wait until the fall?
?
'We seek refuge in Allah.' The Mu'tazila were smart people who let their intellects lead them into absurdities. Are you using your intelligence to get closer to the truth, or to build elaborate justifications for what you already want to believe? Ask Allah to protect you from the same.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
One Motion Cannot Have Two Causes
Proof and refutation of Mu'tazilite view on motion causation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single motion cannot be caused by two independent agents and how the Mu'tazilite position leads to logical absurdities.

Core Idea
One motion, one cause; two causes produce either no motion or absurd multiplicity.

This lesson tackles a fundamental question in kalam: can a single effect have multiple causes? Imam Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite view that one motion could arise from two agents. Using logical reasoning and reductio ad absurdum, he shows that such a claim leads to impossible consequences, defending the principle that every effect has a single cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice the author's use of hypothetical scenarios and logical extrapolation.
2
Understand the initial logical principle
The passage opens with a key principle: if a cause were non-existent, its effect would also be non-existent. This establishes that a cause must be present for the effect to exist.
This principle is used to set up the contradiction.
3
Follow the verification with the example
The author verifies this with a concrete example: a part of a body traverses only one place. If two motions were claimed, the unity of the place suggests only one motion exists.
4
See the reductio ad absurdum
The author then pushes the Mu'tazilite claim to its logical extreme: if two motions could traverse the same place, then a thousand motions could too—an obvious absurdity.
This step demonstrates the power of reductio in kalam arguments.
5
Note the conclusion and critique
The author concludes that the Mu'tazilites' corrupt principles lead them to permit further absurdities, such as one accident in many loci or many accidents of the same genus without effect. He criticizes their disregard for reason and religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينعدم المعلول لو انعدم هو لا يكون علة بالثبوت، ولو كان لما كان به فكانت إحداهما غير موجبة للخروج فلم تكن حركة.
يحققه : أن هذا الجزء ما قطع إلا مكانا وحدا، ولو جاز لك أن تقول: وجدت حركتان لوجود محركين كان لغيرك أن يقول : لا بل وجدت حركة لاتحاد المكان الذي قطع ويقال له: لو جاز وجود حركتين لا يقطع بهما إلا مكان واحد لجاز وجود ألف حركة لا يقطع بهما إلا مكان واحد، وهو يلتزم هنا.
ويقول: لو حرك الجسم ألف نفر وأكثر حدث في كل جزء من أجزائه من الحركات بعدد الفاعلين، والقول بوجود ألف حركة لا يقطع بها إلا مكان واحد تجاهل، ولو جاز أن يخطر أحد هذا بباله لجاز لغيره أن يقول على القلب، ويجوز أن يقطع ألف مكان بحركة واحدة. وهذا كله هذيان، فاقتضت بهم أصولهم الفاسدة إلى أن جوزوا تارة قيام عرض واحد بألف محل وأكثر، وجوزوا تارة قيام ألف عرض من جنس واحد وأكثر بمحل لا أثر لها إلا ما لواحد منها، وهذا كله محال. ومن دأب المعتزلة عدم المبالاة بالانسلاخ عن الدين، وارتكاب ما يأباه العقل وينادي بإحالته ويحكم على قائله بالتجاهل والخروج عن المعارف عند رجائهم نصرة ما هم عليه من الباطل، ودفع ما يتوجه عليهم من الحجج الهادفة لقواعد ما هم عليه من الضلال الآتية على ما يتمسكون به من البدع بالإبطال والاستئصال والرجوع إلى الطريق المستبين والصراط المستقيم خير من الإمعان في المهاوي والمهالك والإيجاف في البوادي والمجاهل، ونعوذ بالله تعالى من اتباع الوساوس والوقوع في ترهات البسابس
The effect is non-existent; if it (the cause) were non-existent, it would not be a cause by affirmation. And if it were, then it would be by it; thus one of them would not be necessitating the emergence, so there would be no motion. He verifies it: This part did not traverse except one place. And if it were permissible for you to say: 'Two motions came into existence due to two movers,' then another could say: 'No, one motion came into existence because of the unity of the place traversed.' And it is said to him: 'If it were permissible for two motions to exist such that only one place is traversed by them, then it would be permissible for a thousand motions to exist such that only one place is traversed by them,' and he commits to this. And he says: 'If a thousand or more persons moved a body, there would occur in each of its parts motions equal to the number of agents. And the statement that a thousand motions exist such that only one place is traversed by them is ignorance. And if it were permissible for anyone to think this, then it would be permissible for another to say the opposite, and it would be permissible for one motion to traverse a thousand places.' All of this is nonsense. So their corrupt principles led them to permit sometimes the subsistence of one accident in a thousand or more loci, and other times the subsistence of a thousand or more accidents of the same genus in a locus with no effect except that of a single one of them, and all of this is impossible. And part of the habit of the Mu'tazilites is lack of concern for stripping oneself of religion and committing what the intellect refuses and declares impossible and judges its speaker to be ignorant and outside of cognitions, when they hope to support the falsehood they are upon and repel the arguments directed against them that undermine the foundations of the misguidance they are upon, which come to invalidate and uproot the innovations they cling to. And returning to the clear path and the straight way is better than persisting in precipices and destructions and agitation in deserts and wildernesses. And we seek refuge with Allah the Exalted from following whisperings and falling into the absurdities of calamities.
Source pages p. 915

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction showing that a single motion cannot be caused by two independent agents. The author begins with a logical statement: if a cause were non-existent, its effect would also be non-existent, so it cannot be a cause by affirmation. Then he verifies this with a concrete example: a part of a body traverses only one place. If someone claims that two motions occur from two movers, the opponent can reply that only one motion occurs because the place is one. Furthermore, if two motions could traverse the same place, then a thousand motions could also traverse it—an absurd consequence. The author then imagines a thousand movers moving a body, resulting in multiple motions in each part, but claiming that a thousand motions occupy the same place is sheer ignorance. If one permitted such a thought, it would lead to the opposite absurdity: one motion traversing a thousand places. This all follows from the Mu'tazilites' corrupt principles, which allow one accident to exist in many loci or many accidents of the same genus in one locus without additional effect. The author concludes by criticizing the Mu'tazilites' disregard for religion and reason, and urges a return to the straight path.

Key Terms
حركة
فاعل
محرك
عرض
محل
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that multiple causes can produce a single effect by cooperation.
Correction: The argument shows that if two causes could produce one motion, then by extension a thousand causes could produce a thousand motions in the same place, leading to absurdity. Thus, a single effect requires a single cause.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does the author derive from the claim that two motions can traverse the same place?
A) That two motions can exist in two different places
B) That a thousand motions could also traverse the same place
C) That motion is eternal
D) That motion is an accident

Key Takeaway
A single motion cannot be caused by two independent agents because it leads to logical absurdities such as infinite motions in one place, affirming the principle that each effect has one cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a success, do you genuinely attribute it solely to Allah, or do you unconsciously treat your own efforts as an equal cause alongside Him? What would it take to remove that mental partnership from your heart?
?
The Mu'tazilites clung to a false principle even when reason and scripture opposed it. Is there any belief or habit in your life that you defend despite clear evidence against it? Will you surrender it to the truth today?
?
If you believe that Allah is the sole creator of every motion and event, why do you still feel anxiety about outcomes that depend on secondary causes? How can this lesson reshape your trust in Allah's sole causality?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Analogy Between Motion and Speech
Refutes Mu'tazilite analogy using ontological distinctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi and Ash'ari theologians reject the Mu'tazilite argument that if one motion can be from two agents, then one speech can be from two speakers.

Core Idea
God's act and the servant's act differ in kind, not degree.

The Mu'tazilites argued by analogy: if one motion can be the act of two agents, then one speech could belong to two speakers, undermining the divine origin of the Qur'an. In this passage, the author dismantles this objection by clarifying the ontological distinction between divine acts and human acts, and by appealing to the doctrines of acquisition (kasb) and the subsistence of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite objection
Start by reading the opening line: the Mu'tazilites claim that if a single act can be from two agents, then a single speech can be from two speakers. This is the challenge.
2
Identify the Maturidi response
The author first dismisses the objection by noting that Maturidis do not consider a single act to be the act of two agents in the same sense. Note the distinction between God's act (an eternal attribute) and the servant's act (a created effect).
The term 'act' is equivocal.
3
Explore the concept of kasb
The author cites Abu Isa al-Burghuth and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who affirm that only God creates acts; the servant acquires them. Understand how 'speaker' refers to the one in whom speech subsists, not its creator.
Acquisition means the servant's involvement is in the locus, not in creation.
4
Analyze the counter-argument about necessary motion
The passage then addresses an objection: in necessary motions, the agent is the creator and the moved object is its locus. The author argues that two essences cannot be described by one motion, just as blackness cannot subsist in two loci.
This reinforces that one attribute cannot inhere in two subjects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يصولون على خصومهم بقولهم : لوجاز أن يكون فعل واحد فعلاً لفاعلين جاز أن يكون قول واحد قولاً لقائلين . يبطل بهذا أيضًا على ما قررنا أن الحركة الواحدة عندهم فعل لفاعلين ولم ينفعهم التجاهل ثم نقول : إن هذا الكلام لا يتوجه علينا فإنا لا نقول إن فعلاً واحدًا يكون فعلاً لفاعلين البتة، فإن ما هو فعل الله تعالى هو صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته وليس بفعل للعبد وما هو فعل للعبد ليس بفعل الله تعالى بل هو مفعوله، ثم الفعل اسم عام وله أسماء وأنواع ، فإن كان ما خلقه الله تعالى من فعل العبد ضربًا فهو معقول الله تعالى وفعل العبد باعتبار اسم جنسه و ضربه باعتبار اسم نوعه، وكذا لو خلق قول العبد فهو مفعول الله تعالى وهو فعل العبد باعتبار اسم جنسه وقوله باعتبار اسم نوعه، وكذا على قول أبي عيسى البرغوث وأبي حسن الأشعري لا يتوجه الإلزام فإنهما لا يجعلان فعلاً واحد لفاعلين بل لا فعل إلا لله تعالى، فأما العبد فله الكسب وقول العبد اسم لكسبه فيكون قوله فعلاً الله تعالى كسبًا للعبد، ثم ما هو من الأسماء للكسب يكون راجعا إلى من قام به الكسب فإن كان ذلك ضربًا فالضارب من قام به لا من خلقه والمتحرك من قامت به الحركة لا من خلقها، و القائل من قام به القول لا من خلقه، ولا يتصور قيام شيء من هذه المعاني إلا بمحل واحد فلا يوصف به اثنان، فأما الاتصاف بكونه فاعلاً ليس من شرطه قيام الفعل بالفاعل عندهم، ويحتجون عليكم في ذلك بالحركة والصفات الضرورية، فإن الفاعل للحركة الضرورية خالقها والمتحرك بها محلها، ولا يتصور اتصاف ذاتين بأنهما متحركان بحركة واحدة لاستحالة قياسها بذاتين، وكذا هذا في السواد فإن
And among what they assault their opponents with is their saying: 'If it were permissible for one act to be an act of two agents, then it would be permissible for one speech to be a speech of two speakers.' This is also invalidated based on what we established that one motion according to them is an act of two agents, and ignorance did not benefit them. Then we say: This speech does not apply to us, for we certainly do not say that one act is an act of two agents at all. For what is the act of Allah the Exalted is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, and it is not an act of the servant; and what is an act of the servant is not an act of Allah the Exalted but rather His effect (maf'ul). Then 'act' is a general name, and it has names and types. If what Allah the Exalted creates of the servant's act is striking, then it is the intelligible of Allah the Exalted and the servant's act in respect of the name of its genus, and his striking in respect of the name of its type. Likewise, if He creates the speech of the servant, it is the effect of Allah the Exalted and it is the act of the servant in respect of the name of its genus, and his speech in respect of the name of its type. And likewise according to the saying of Abu Isa al-Burghuth and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the charge does not apply, for they do not make one act for two agents; rather, there is no act except for Allah the Exalted. As for the servant, he has acquisition (kasb), and the speech of the servant is a name for his acquisition. So his speech is an act of Allah the Exalted and an acquisition of the servant. Then that which is among the names for acquisition refers to the one in whom the acquisition subsists. If that is striking, then the striker is the one in whom it subsists, not the one who created it; the mover is the one in whom motion subsists, not the one who created it; and the speaker is the one in whom speech subsists, not the one who created it. It is inconceivable for any of these meanings to subsist except in one locus, so it cannot be attributed to two. As for being described as an agent, its condition is not subsistence of the act in the agent according to them. And they argue against you in that by motion and necessary attributes. For the agent of necessary motion is its creator, and the one moved by it is its locus; and it is inconceivable for two essences to be described as both being moved by one motion because it is impossible for it to be measured to two essences. And likewise this in blackness, for...
Source pages p. 916

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazilites and a detailed response. The Mu'tazilites argue by analogy: if one motion can be the act of two agents, then one speech can belong to two speakers, implying that the Qur'an could be the speech of both God and the Prophet, which is unacceptable. The author first notes that this argument fails against the earlier established point that one motion is their act (for Mu'tazilites). Then he explains that the objection does not apply to the Maturidi position because they do not claim that a single act is the act of two agents in the same sense. God's act is an eternal attribute in His essence, while the servant's act is not God's act but His creation (maf'ul). The term 'act' is general; when God creates a servant's striking, it is God's creation (ma'qul) and the servant's act in genus, and the servant's striking in type. Similarly for speech. The author then cites Abu Isa al-Burghuth and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who also avoid the charge by denying that any act is from two agents; only God creates acts, and the servant acquires (kasb) them. The names like 'striker' or 'speaker' refer to the one in whom the attribute subsists, not the creator. It is impossible for one attribute to subsist in two loci. He then addresses a further objection about necessary motions: the agent of necessary motion is its creator, but the one moved is its locus; two essences cannot share one motion. The passage breaks off mid-sentence at 'فإن', likely continuing with an example of blackness.

قول
كسب
مفعول
فاعل
صفة أزلية
أبو عيسى البرغوث
أبو حسن الأشعري

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Maturidi/Ash'ari position collapses God and the servant into one agent for a single act, but the distinction between creation and acquisition shows they are not acting in the same sense.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's act is an eternal attribute, while the servant's act is a created effect or acquisition; they are ontologically distinct and not two agents of the same act.

Checkpoint
How do Maturidis and Ash'aris avoid the Mu'tazilite charge that one speech would then belong to two speakers?
A) They deny that speech is an act altogether
B) They say God's act is an eternal attribute and the servant's act is His creation, differing in kind
C) They claim that speech is not an act but a substance
D) They affirm that the Qur'an is created

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite analogy fails because divine and human acts are ontologically distinct, and speech as an attribute cannot subsist in two loci.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak, do you remember that your speech is created by Allah and that you are merely the locus in which it subsists? How does that awareness change the care you take with your words?
?
You likely believe that your actions are your own responsibility, yet this lesson insists they are created by Allah and only acquired by you. Does this distinction make you feel more humble about your achievements or more accountable for your sins? Examine your heart honestly.
?
If the Mu'tazilite analogy were true, the Prophet (peace be upon him) could be considered a speaker of the Qur'an alongside Allah. How does the refutation in this lesson protect your conviction that the Qur'an is purely Allah's speech, not mixed with human origin? Let that reshape your reverence when you recite.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Distinction Between Divine Creation and Human Acquisition
Nasafi distinguishes divine creation from human acquisition using the stone motion example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that God creates actions but humans acquire them, thus blame falls on the human for acquisition, not on God for creation.

Core Idea
God creates the action; humans acquire it—blame is on the acquisition, not the creation.

Nasafi continues his refutation by distinguishing between God's creation (ikhtirā') and human acquisition (kasb). He explains that while God creates the action, the human is responsible for acquiring it and thus deserves blame or praise. To illustrate, he uses the example of a stone's motion: the same motion can be obedience for one mover and disobedience for another, showing that moral responsibility depends on the agent's intention and command, not on the act's createdness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the distinction Nasafi makes between God's action (creation) and human action (acquisition).
The passage uses technical terms like مفعول (effect) and اختراع (creation).
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice that Nasafi says God has an action and creation, but He is not blamed for creation; the human is blamed for his own action (acquisition). This is the central point.
The phrase 'له فعلاً ولا اختراع له' indicates that God creates but the human has an act without creation.
3
Analyze the stone motion example
Nasafi presents the example of a stone's movement. If one mover is commanded to move it and another is prohibited, the same motion can be obedience for one and disobedience for the other. This shows that the moral quality depends on the agent's relationship to the command, not on the motion itself.
The example refutes the idea that if God creates an action, He is responsible for its moral quality.
4
Reflect on the implications for divine justice
Consider how this distinction preserves God's justice: He creates the action but does not incur blame because the human freely acquires it and is responsible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم هل مفعول الله تعالى وهو فعله فيذم على فعله كما هو عندكم شيء وذات وعين وموجود ويذم على وجوده دون شيئيته وذاتيته وعينيته، على أنا إذا أثبتنا بالدليل أن له فعلاً ولا اختراع له فيذم هو على فعله ولا يذم الله تعالى على اختراعه لما نبين بعد هذا - إن شاء الله تعالى كما يذم عندكم على وجود فعله دون شيئيته على أن هذا يلزم أبا الهذيل وأبا موسى المردار حيث جعلا حركة الحجر فعلاً للمحركين، ولو كان أحدهما مأمورًا بالتحريك والآخر منهيًا عنه فتكون الحركة في ذاتها شيئًا واحدًا وهي طاعة ومعصية يحمد المطيع منهما عليها ويثاب ويذم العاصي منهما عليها ويعاقب، وكذا لو كان المحركين الريح كانت الحركة في نفسها فعلاً الله تعالى ويحمد عليه وفعلاً للمحرك المنهى عنه ومعصية منه يذم عليها ويعاقب، وإن لم تكن هي متجزئة في نفسها، والله الموفق.
Then we say to them: Is the effect of God, which is His action, such that He is blamed for His action, as according to you it is a thing, an essence, an entity, and an existent, and you blame for its existence without its thingness, essentiality, and entityness? But when we have proven by evidence that He has an action and not creation, then he [the servant] is blamed for his action, and God is not blamed for His creation, as we will explain after this—if God wills—just as you blame for the existence of His action without its thingness. Moreover, this applies to Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu Musa al-Mirdar, who made the movement of the stone an action of the movers. If one of them was commanded to move and the other prohibited, then the movement in itself is one thing, and it is obedience and disobedience; the obedient one is praised and rewarded, and the disobedient one is blamed and punished. Similarly, if the mover were the wind, the movement in itself would be an act of God and He would be praised for it, and an act of the prohibited mover and a sin from him, for which he would be blamed and punished, even though it is not divisible in itself. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 922

Commentary
This passage is a further refutation, making a distinction between God's creation (ikhtirā') of an action and the human's acquisition (kasb) of it. Nasafi argues that God's action is His creation, but the human action is something else—the human's own act. God is not blamed for creating the action; rather, blame falls on the human for his acquisition. He then uses the example of a stone's motion: according to Abu al-Hudhayl and al-Mirdar, the motion of a stone is the action of the mover. If one mover is commanded to move it and another is prohibited, the same motion can be obedience for one and disobedience for the other, despite being metaphysically one indivisible accident. This shows that the same action can be attributed differently depending on the agent's intention and command. Nasafi concludes that this principle undermines the opponent's objection.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates all actions, then He is responsible for evil and sin.
Correction: God creates the action's existence, but the human acquires it through intention and choice; thus moral responsibility lies with the human, not God.

Checkpoint

A) Because human actions are not real
B) Because God only creates the action, but the human acquires it
C) Because God does not know them
D) Because actions are not created by God

Key Takeaway
The distinction between divine creation and human acquisition preserves moral responsibility: God creates the act, but humans acquire it and bear blame or praise.

Causality and Created Things

The Objection and its First Refutation
Nasafi presents the opponents' argument and his answer using proof and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' claim that the creator of evil must be evil, and Nasafi's foundational response based on divine exclusive creativity and consensus of rational people.

Core Idea
Creating evil does not make one evil; God's transcendence is established by consensus.

The opponents argue that whoever causes evil is evil, and since God causes disbelief (evil), He would be evil. Nasafi first counters by reiterating that God alone creates all events, and rational consensus affirms that God is not evil. Anyone who claims otherwise is a disbeliever. This lesson presents the objection and the initial answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the translation. Notice the opponents' logic: if someone creates evil, they are evil; God creates disbelief (evil); therefore God would be evil.
The opponents also strengthen their argument with a comparison between faith and disbelief.
2
Identify Nasafi's two-part response
Nasafi first reminds that God alone originates every event (proof from earlier). Second, he cites the consensus of rational people that God is not evil; anyone who says otherwise is a disbeliever.
These two points together show that the one who brings evil is not evil.
3
Reflect on the combination
Ask yourself: How do the proof of exclusive creativity and the consensus work together? The proof establishes God as the sole creator, and the consensus establishes that He is not evil. Thus, creating evil does not entail being evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: من أوجد الشر فهو شرير، والكفر شر فلو أوجده الله تعالى لكان شريراً، والله تعالى منزه عن هذا الوصف. وربما يقررون هذا ويقولون: الإيمان خير وصلاح والدعاء إليه خير والفاعل للإيمان أصلح من الداعي، والكفر شر والدعاء إليه شر، فيجب أن يكون خالق الكفر شر وأفسد من هذا الداعي. والجواب عنه: أنا قد أقمنا الدلالة على أن الله تعالى هو المخترع لكل حادث ولا قدرة لغيره على الاختراع، وانعقد إجماع العقلاء أن الله تعالى ليس بشرير، ومن زعم أنه شرير فهو كافر فظهر من مجموع الدليلين أن موجد ما هو شر ليس بشرير. والذي يهدم عليهم هذه القاعدة أنهم يزعمون أن ما هو الكفر والظلم والقبح مما يصح من المجانين والأطفال ويكون ذلك شرا بالوجود، ولا يصح أن يوصف واحد منهم بأنه شرير. فدل أن وجود ما هو شر من ذات لا يوجب كون موجده شريرا ولا كونه شرًا من الداعي. ومما يبطل أيضا كلامهم : أنهم لما جعلوا موجد الكفر شرًا من الكفر والله تعالى خلق الكافر الذي هو شر من الكفر ، ولم يصر بخلق ما هو شر من الكفر شريراً ، كيف يصير بخلق الكفر الذي هو أدون من الكافر في كونه شرا شريرا؟ وظهر بهذا بطلان قولهم : إن موجد الشر شرير.
If they say: Whoever brings evil is evil, and disbelief is evil, so if God caused it He would be evil, and God Almighty is transcendent above this description. And perhaps they establish this and say: Faith is good and righteous and calling to it is good, and the doer of faith is more righteous than the caller. Disbelief is evil and calling to it is evil, so the creator of disbelief must be more evil and more corrupt than this caller. The answer to it: We have established the proof that God Almighty is the originator of every event and no one else has power to originate, and the consensus of rational people is that God Almighty is not evil, and whoever claims He is evil is a disbeliever. So from the combination of the two proofs it appears that the one who brings what is evil is not evil. And what demolishes this principle for them is that they claim that what is disbelief, injustice, and ugliness is something that can occur from the insane and children, and that is evil in existence, yet it is not valid to describe any one of them as evil. So it indicates that the existence of something evil from an entity does not necessitate that its bringer is evil, nor that it is evil from the caller. And what also invalidates their statement: that they made the creator of disbelief more evil than disbelief, and God Almighty created the disbeliever who is more evil than disbelief, and He did not become evil by creating what is more evil than disbelief; how would He become evil by creating disbelief, which is inferior to the disbeliever in being evil? And by this the falsehood of their statement that the bringer of evil is evil is made clear.
Source pages p. 931

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponents argue that whoever causes evil (sharr) is evil (sharīr); since God causes disbelief (kufr), He would be evil. They strengthen it by comparing faith and disbelief: the doer of faith is better than the caller, so the creator of disbelief is worse than the one who calls to it. Nasafi's answer: (1) We have already proven that God alone originates all events, and there is consensus that God is not evil; anyone who claims He is evil is a disbeliever. So, despite creating what is evil, God is not evil. (2) He undermines their principle by noting that insane people and children can produce evil acts (disbelief, injustice, ugliness) but we never call them evil. Thus, producing evil does not make the producer evil. (3) A further argument: the opponents claim the creator of disbelief is more evil than disbelief itself. Yet God creates the disbeliever (who is more evil than disbelief), and God is not evil; so how could creating the lesser evil (disbelief) make Him evil? Hence, their statement is false.

Key Terms
الشر
الشرير
الكفر
المخترع
إجماع العقلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates evil, He must be evil.
Correction: Nasafi shows that God creates all things, yet rational consensus affirms He is not evil; creating something evil does not make the creator evil.

Checkpoint
What two things does Nasafi use to show that the one who brings evil is not evil?
A) Proof that God alone creates and consensus of rational people
B) Example of children and the insane
C) Comparison with the disbeliever
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
God creates everything, including evil, but He is not evil; this is known through proof and rational consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face injustice or suffering, do you ever secretly question God’s goodness, even though the consensus of rational people says He is not evil? What would it look like for you to internalize that conviction today?
?
The opponents claimed that the doer of faith is better than the caller, but does your own sense of self-righteousness rely on comparing yourself to others? How can you root your identity in God’s transcendence rather than your own moral scorecard?
?
If you truly believed that God creating evil does not make Him evil, how would that change the way you react to the evil in your own heart—your own sins? Would it free you to rely more on His mercy and less on your own excuses?

Causality and Created Things

Further Refutations: Examples and Comparative Argument
Nasafi provides two additional refutations: the example of children/insane and the comparative argument about the disbeliever.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional arguments from Nasafi that demolish the claim that the creator of evil is evil: the example of children and the insane, and the comparative argument from the disbeliever.

Core Idea
Even humans produce evil without being evil; and God creates greater evil without being evil, so how would lesser evil make Him evil?

After the initial refutation, Nasafi presents two further arguments that completely undermine the opponents' principle. First, he points out that children and the insane perform acts of disbelief and injustice, which are evil, yet we never call them evil. Second, he uses a comparative argument: the opponents claim the creator of disbelief is more evil than disbelief itself, but God creates the disbeliever (who is more evil than disbelief) without becoming evil, so creating mere disbelief cannot make Him evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage slowly
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Note that there are two distinct arguments.
2
First argument: children and insane produce evil
Nasafi says that children and the insane can produce acts of disbelief, injustice, and ugliness, which are evil in existence, yet we never describe them as evil. This shows that producing evil does not make one evil.
Ask yourself: If a child does something evil, do we call the child evil? No. So why would God be called evil for creating evil?
3
Second argument: Comparison with the disbeliever
The opponents claim the creator of disbelief is more evil than disbelief itself. But God creates the disbeliever, who is more evil than disbelief, and God is not evil. Therefore, creating disbelief (which is less evil than the disbeliever) cannot make God evil.
This is a logical reductio ad absurdum.
4
Synthesize the two arguments
Both arguments show that the opponents' principle 'whoever creates evil is evil' is false. The example of children/insane provides a counterexample; the comparative argument shows inconsistency.

Source Text

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Absurd Consequence of Human Creation of Actions
Reductio ad absurdum against human creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the absurd consequences of the Mu'tazilite view that humans create their own actions, namely the implication that a pious servant would be better than God.

Core Idea
Claiming humans create actions leads to the blasphemy that a righteous servant is better than God.

This passage presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of human creation of actions. By following the logical implications, the author shows that such a view necessarily implies that a pious human's deeds are superior to God's, which is clearly false. The author also notes how the Mu'tazila try to evade this conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the contrast between what is attributed to God's power and what to the servant's power.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
The author argues that if humans create their own good deeds, then a pious human's actions (prayer, charity, etc.) are better than God's actions (creating evil, disease, etc.), making the human superior to God.
This is the core refutation: the Mu'tazilite view leads to kufr.
3
Observe the Mu'tazila response
The author notes that the Mu'tazila are aware of this implication, so they avoid focusing on good deeds and instead cling to evil deeds to promote their falsehood.
This shows their dishonesty in argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفاعلين بتفاضل الأفعال فينبغي أن يكون كل عبد مطيع أفضل من الله تعالى وأحسن فعلاً وأرفع درجة لعلو رتبة فعله على فعل الله تعالى، وانحطاط رتبة فعله عز وجل عن أفعال العباد، ويكون في مقدور العبد من المحاسن ما ليس يماثل ذلك أو يقاربه في مقدور الله تعالى، والقول به قول لا خفاء بما فيه، فكان مقدور الله تعالى هو إبليس والقردة والأقذار والأنتان والزمانة والعمى والشلل والأمراض والموت والمصائب ومقدور العبد الصوم والصلاة والحج والإقراض والتصديق واعتقاد التوحيد والإفضال إلى المحتاجين والإحسان إليهم والوفاء بالعهود وأداء الأمانات والجود والسماحة، وغير ذلك من الفضائل الداخلة تحت قدرة العباد، تقدر كل واحد من الفاعلين بمقدور، وذهب بما خلق وفاز العبد بالقسم الأحسن والحظ الأجزل، وقصرت قدرة القديم عن ذلك، ولعلم المعتزلة بهذا الإلزام يعرضون عن جانب المحاسن صفحًا ويتشبثون بالمقابح ليتمكنوا عسى من ترويج باطلهم على ضعفاء عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك بكرمه وفضله وهو ذو الفضل العظيم.
The doers differ in actions, so it would be necessary that every obedient servant is better than God Almighty and more excellent in action and higher in rank due to the elevation of the rank of his action over God's action, and the degradation of His rank, exalted, over the actions of servants. And there would be within the servant's power of good deeds what is not similar to that or near to it in God's power. And to say this is a saying whose content is not hidden. So God's power would be [such that] He creates Iblis, monkeys, filth, stench, chronic illness, blindness, paralysis, diseases, death, and calamities. And the servant's power would be fasting, prayer, Hajj, lending, charity, belief in tawhid, beneficence to the needy, kindness to them, fulfilling covenants, fulfilling trusts, generosity, and magnanimity, and other virtues that fall under the power of servants. Each of the two doers would be measured by what is within their power. And He would have wasted what He created, and the servant would win the superior portion and the greater lot, and the power of the Eternal would fall short of that. And because the Mu'tazila recognize this implication, they turn away from the side of good deeds and cling to evil deeds, so that they might perhaps succeed in promoting their falsehood among the weak. May God protect us from that by His generosity and grace, and He is Possessor of Great Grace.
Source pages p. 938

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own actions. The author demonstrates the absurd consequences of that view. The argument proceeds as follows: If humans are the creators of their own actions, then the actions of a pious servant—such as prayer, fasting, charity, belief in tawhid—are superior to the actions of God, since God's power would then only produce evil things like Iblis, monkeys, diseases, and death. This would imply that a human is better than God and has a higher rank, which is blasphemous. The author notes that the Mu'tazila themselves are aware of this implication, so they avoid focusing on good deeds and instead emphasize evil deeds in their arguments, hoping to trick the ignorant. The passage ends with a prayer for protection from such error.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
خلق الأفعال
مقدور
تقدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that denying human creation of actions means God is the doer of evil.
Correction: The author is refuting the consequences of the Mu'tazilite belief, not affirming evil to God. The point is that if humans create their own good deeds, then God would only create evil, which is absurd. The correct belief is that God creates all actions, both good and evil, but He is not pleased with evil.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence of the Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own actions, according to the author?
A) Humans would be equal to God in power.
B) A righteous human would be better than God, since God creates evil while humans create good.
C) God would be responsible for human sins.
D) Humans would not be accountable for their actions.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own actions leads to the absurd conclusion that a righteous human is better than God, which is why the author refutes it.

?
The Mu'tazila turned away from considering good deeds because it would have refuted their position. In your own life, are there truths about God that you avoid because they would demand too much humility or change from you?
?
If you truly believed that every good thing in your life—from prayer to charity—is entirely an act of God's creation within you, would it change the way you thank Him? Or the way you see others who fall short?

Invalidating the Doctrine of Generated Acts (Tawallud)
Nasafi presents Qadari theory of generated acts and initial refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qadari doctrine of tawallud and Nasafi's initial refutation based on the locus of human power.

Core Idea
Generated acts are not acts of the servant because they occur outside his locus of power.

This passage from Imam Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla introduces the debate on generated acts (tawallud). After establishing that human power only extends to actions directly within one's body, Nasafi argues that effects like arrow flight or breaking a vessel cannot be attributed to human creation. He then presents the opposing Qadari view which claims these are human-generated. Understanding this sets the stage for deeper arguments on causality and divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Nasafi first states his premise then presents the Qadari position.
The passage is incomplete but forms a coherent unit.
2
Identify key concepts
Identify tawallud, qudrat takhliq, iktisab, and mahall qudra. Understand their meanings.
3
Examine the examples
List the examples: wood movement, animal pain, arrow flight, breaking, tearing. What common feature do they share? They occur outside the agent's body.
4
Understand the Qadari claim
The Qadari assert that humans create these effects, calling them generated acts. Note how this contradicts Nasafi's premise.
5
Reflect on the incomplete argument
The passage breaks off, but the refutation is based on the principle that only God creates outside the human locus.
The full refutation continues in later pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إبطال القول بالتولد : فإذا ثبت أن ليس للعبد قدرة التخليق ولا اكتساب إلا لما يحل محل قدرته ثبت أن ما يوجد في الخشبة من الحركة عقيب اعتماد الرجل عليها، وما يوجد من الألم في الحيوان عقيب ضرب الرجل إياه وأشباه ذلك ليس بفعل للعباد لا بطريق التخليق ولا بطريق الاكتساب. وزعم جمهور القدرية أن ما يحدث من هذا الجنس في المحال عقيب أسباب يفعلها الإنسان في حيزه كلها أفعال الإنسان وهو خلقها لا صنع الله تعالى في شيء من ذلك، ويسمونها الأفعال المتولدة، فكان مرور السهم وحركاته وإصابته الهدف وإصابته الحيوان وما يحدث فيه من الانجراح والآلام والموت كل ذلك متولدًا من تحريك الرامي يده بالقوس ونزعه إياها، والعبد خالق كل ذلك ومكتسبه، وكذا الانكسار في الأواني والانخراق في الثياب. وأجاز بشر بن
Discourse on invalidating the doctrine of generated acts (tawallud): When it is established that the servant has no power of creation nor acquisition except for what enters the locus of his power, it is established that what occurs in the piece of wood of movement after the man's treading on it, and what occurs of pain in the animal after the man's striking it, and similar things, are not an act of the servants, neither by way of creation nor by way of acquisition. And the majority of the Qadariyya claim that what occurs of this kind in the loci after causes that the human does are all in his domain, all of them are acts of the human, and he creates them, and God has no creation in any of that. And they call them 'generated acts' (al-af‘al al-mutawallida). So the passage of the arrow, its movements, its hitting the target, its hitting the animal, and what occurs in it of wounding, pains, and death—all that is generated from the archer's movement of his hand with the bow and his drawing it, and the servant is the creator of all that and its acquirer. Similarly, the breaking of vessels and the tearing of clothes. And Bishr bin [al-Mu‘tamir?] allowed...
Source pages p. 943

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on invalidating the doctrine of tawallud (generated acts). Nasafi first states the premise that has been established: the servant has no power of creation or acquisition except for what occurs within the locus of his power (i.e., directly in his own body). Therefore, things like the movement of a piece of wood when someone steps on it, or pain in an animal when struck, are not acts of the servant—neither by creation nor acquisition. Then he presents the Qadari (Mu'tazili) opposing view: they claim that such effects (like arrow flight, wounding, breaking) are all generated from the human's action and are the human's creation, not God's. They call them 'generated acts' (mutawallida). Nasafi lists examples: the arrow's movement, hitting, causing pain and death—all generated from the archer's movement. Similarly, breaking of vessels and tearing of clothes. He mentions that Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir (a Mu'tazili) allowed some form of this. The passage breaks off mid-sentence, but it sets up the Qadari position that Nasafi will refute.

قدرة تخليق
اكتساب
أفعال متولدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Generated acts (tawallud) are human acts independent of God's creation.
Correction: Nasafi argues that these acts cannot be human acts at all because they occur outside the human's locus of power. Only God creates such effects.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why cannot the movement of a piece of wood when stepped on be considered the servant's act?

B) Because it occurs outside the servant's locus of power
C) Because wood is inanimate
D) Because the Qadari say so

Key Takeaway
The Qadari claim that humans create effects outside their bodies contradicts the principle that human power is limited to their own locus; thus, generated acts cannot be attributed to human creation.

?
The Qadari claimed humans create all generated acts, effectively making themselves partners with Allah in creation. What would change in your daily gratitude and reliance on Allah if you truly believed that even the effects of your actions are not your doing?

Refutation of Tawallud: The Qadariyya Claim on Generated Acts
Nasafi states the premise and presents the Qadariyya and Bishr's view on generated acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qadariyya theory of generated acts (tawallud) and why Nasafi rejects it as inconsistent with the limits of human power.

Core Idea
Generated acts exceed human power, so they cannot be human creations.

Nasafi opens his refutation of tawallud by laying down a crucial premise: humans can only create or acquire what is directly within their power. He then presents the view of the Qadariyya (Muʿtazila) who claim that effects like an arrow's flight or breaking a vessel are human acts, created by the servant. This lesson introduces both the Maturidi premise and the Qadariyya counter-claim, setting the stage for the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: a premise, a conclusion, then a report of the opposing view.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify Nasafi's premise
Find the sentence stating that the servant has no power of creation (takhlīq) or acquisition (iktisāb) except for what falls within his ability. This is the foundation of the argument.
This premise comes directly from earlier discussions on human capability.
3
Understand the Qadariyya claim
Identify the claim that generated acts (al-afʿāl al-mutawallida) are created by humans, not God. Note the examples: arrow's flight, hitting the target, wounds, breaking, tearing.
The Qadariyya extend human power beyond direct acts.
4
Examine Bishr's extreme view
Notice that Bishr ibn al-Muʿtamir goes further, including perceptions (hearing, sight) and qualities (colors, tastes) as human creations. This shows the logical extreme of the theory.
Nasafi will refute this later in the text.
5
Reflect on the overall structure
Consider how Nasafi sets up the refutation: first stating what is not a human act, then presenting the opposing view to be refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إبطال القول بالتولد : فإذا ثبت أن ليس للعبد قدرة التخليق ولا اكتساب إلا لما يحل محل قدرته ثبت أن ما يوجد في الخشبة من الحركة عقيب اعتماد الرجل عليها، وما يوجد من الألم في الحيوان عقيب ضرب الرجل إياه وأشباه ذلك ليس بفعل للعباد لا بطريق التخليق ولا بطريق الاكتساب. وزعم جمهور القدرية أن ما يحدث من هذا الجنس في المحال عقيب أسباب يفعلها الإنسان في حيزه كلها أفعال الإنسان وهو خلقها لا صنع الله تعالى في شيء من ذلك، ويسمونها الأفعال المتولدة، فكان مرور السهم وحركاته وإصابته الهدف وإصابته الحيوان وما يحدث فيه من الانجراح والآلام والموت كل ذلك متولدًا من تحريك الرامي يده بالقوس ونزعه إياها، والعبد خالق كل ذلك ومكتسبه، وكذا الانكسار في الأواني والانخراق في الثياب. وأجاز بشر بن المعتمر أحد رؤسائهم أن يكون السمع والبصر وما وراء ذلك من أنواع الإدراك أنواع الألوان والطعوم والروائح متولدة عن فعل الإنسان وكل ذلك يكون مخلوقا له مخترعًا من جهته لا صنع الله تعالى فيه.
The discourse on refuting the claim of generation (tawallud): If it is established that the servant has no power of creation (takhlīq) nor acquisition (iktisāb) except for what falls within the scope of his power, then it is established that what occurs in the wood of movement following the man's treading on it, and what occurs of pain in the animal following the man's striking it, and similar things, are not an act of the servants, neither by way of creation nor by way of acquisition. The majority of the Qadariyya claim that what occurs of this kind in places following causes that a human does, all of them in his domain are acts of the human, and he creates them; there is no act of God the Exalted in any of that, and they call them the generated acts (al-afʿāl al-mutawallida). Thus, the arrow's flight, its movements, its hitting the target, its hitting the animal, and what occurs in it of wounds, pains, and death — all that is generated from the archer's moving his hand with the bow and his drawing it, and the servant is the creator of all that and its acquirer. Likewise, breaking in vessels and tearing in clothes. And Bishr ibn al-Muʿtamir, one of their leaders, allowed that hearing, sight, and beyond that of the types of perception, the types of colors, tastes, and smells, are generated from the human's act, and all that is created by him, invented from his side; there is no act of God the Exalted in it.
Source pages p. 944

Commentary
This passage is the opening of a refutation of the theory of generated acts (tawallud). Nasafi begins by stating a premise: if it is established that the servant has no power of creation or acquisition except for what falls within his capability (i.e., his voluntary actions that he can directly bring about), then it follows that phenomena such as movement in wood from treading, or pain in an animal from striking, are not acts of the servant, neither by creation nor by acquisition. This is because these effects are not directly within the servant's power. Then Nasafi reports the claim of the majority of the Qadariyya (the Muʿtazila), who assert that such generated events are indeed acts of the human, created by him, and God has no part in them. They call these 'generated acts' (al-afʿāl al-mutawallida). Examples include the arrow's flight, hitting the target, causing wounds and pain, breaking vessels, tearing clothes. The human is considered the creator and acquirer of all these. Then Nasafi mentions Bishr ibn al-Muʿtamir, an extreme Qadari leader, who extended generation to include perceptions like hearing, sight, and qualities of colors, tastes, and smells, all created by the human, not by God. Nasafi presents these views to refute them, asserting that such generated things are not human acts.

Key Terms
تولد
الأفعال المتولدة
قدرة التخليق
اكتساب
القدرية
بشر بن المعتمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Muʿtazila only claim that generated acts are caused by humans, not created by them.
Correction: The passage clearly states the Muʿtazila claim that humans create (khalq) and invent (ikhtirāʿ) these acts, with no act of God in them.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi claim is established if the servant has power only over what falls within his capability?
A) Generated acts are created by the servant.
B) Generated acts are not acts of the servant.
C) Generated acts are acts of God by obligation.
D) Generated acts are uncaused.

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya claim that generated acts like an arrow's flight are human creations, but Nasafi argues they exceed human power and thus are not human acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish a task—like writing or building something—do you ever pause to acknowledge that the final outcome, including its effects on others, is ultimately brought about by Allah, not by your own skill? How can you cultivate a habit of attributing all consequences to Him alone?

Think of a time when you caused harm unintentionally, like breaking something or hurting someone's feelings. Did you consider that the resulting damage was beyond your control, created by Allah, or did you feel guilty as if you had created that harm? How does knowing the Maturidi perspective on generated acts free you from misplaced self-blame?
?
The Qadariyya claimed that even your sense perceptions—seeing a color or tasting food—are generated by your own action. Do you ever act as if your perceptions are self-generated, forgetting that Allah is the One who gives you sight and taste? What would change in your gratitude if you recognized His direct creation in every sensory experience?

Refutation of Obligation upon God
Nasafi refutes al-Nazzam and al-Qalānisi's theory of obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the doctrine of obligation (ījāb) upon God is considered corrupt in Sunni theology, as it implies divine compulsion and undermines God's omnipotence.

Core Idea
Obligating God strips Him of sovereignty — the ultimate theological error.

The question of how God relates to secondary causes divided early theologians. Al-Nazzam and al-Qalānisi attempted to preserve divine agency by claiming that God creates effects necessarily when their causes occur, but their solution backfired. In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi exposes the fatal flaw: this view turns God into a compelled agent, which contradicts His nature as absolutely free and omnipotent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice that al-Nasafi first presents the views before refuting them.
2
Identify the two similar views
Al-Nazzam and al-Qalānisi both say God creates generated acts by obligation. What is the difference between them? Al-Nasafi says there is none.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Nasafi argues that obligation on God implies compulsion and that humans could oblige God. Think about why this is theologically unacceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم النظام أن ما يسمى الأفعال المتولدة كل ذلك فعل الله تعالى بإيجاب الخلقة أي أن الله تعالى خلق الشخص الحيواني على وجه يوجب أن يخلق الله تعالى فيه الألم عند الضرب والسهم على وجه يوجب أن يخلق الله تعالى فيه المرور عند الرمي وكذا الزجاج مع الانكسار. وحكى عن أبي العباس القلانسي أن كل ذلك فعل الله تعالى بإيجاب الطبع وهو قريب من مذهب النظام بل لا فرق بينهما، وحاصل المذهبين استحالة انعدام ذلك عند وجود ما هو سببه كما هو مذهب أهل الطبائع إلا أن أولئك يضيفون ذلك إلى طبيعة المحل فحسب، وهما يضيفان إلى الله تعالى ولكن بإيجاب الخلقة والطبع، والقول بالإيجاب على الله قول باضطراره، ويؤدي إلى أن من فعل سببًا في محل يسير موجبًا على الله تعالى أن يفعل ذلك المتولد في المحل بحيث لا يكون له قدرة الامتناع، والقول به ظاهر الفساد بادي العوار.
And al-Nazzam claimed that what are called generated acts — all that is the act of God the Exalted by obligation of nature (ījāb al-khilqa), meaning that God the Exalted created the animal person in such a way that it necessitates that God the Exalted creates pain in it at the time of striking, and the arrow in such a way that it necessitates that God the Exalted creates passage through it at the time of shooting, and likewise glass with breaking. And it was narrated from Abū al-ʿAbbās al-Qalānisī that all that is the act of God the Exalted by obligation of nature (ījāb al-ṭabʿ), and it is close to al-Nazzam's doctrine; indeed, there is no difference between them. The summary of the two doctrines is the impossibility of the non-occurrence of that when its cause exists, as is the doctrine of the naturalists (ahl al-ṭabāʼiʿ), except that those (naturalists) attribute that to the nature of the substrate alone, while these two attribute it to God the Exalted but by obligation of creation and nature. And the statement of obligation upon God is a statement of His being compelled, and it leads to the conclusion that whoever performs a cause in a substrate for a moment obliges God the Exalted to perform that generated act in the substrate such that He has no power to refrain. And this statement is clearly corrupt and obviously flawed.
Source pages p. 945

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the views of al-Nazzam and Abu al-ʿAbbas al-Qalānisi regarding generated acts. al-Nazzam claimed that generated acts are all acts of God, but by 'obligation of nature' (ījāb al-khilqa): God created the animal in such a way that necessarily He creates pain when struck, and the arrow such that He creates movement when shot, and glass such that He creates breaking. Similarly, al-Qalānisī said it is by 'obligation of nature' (ījāb al-ṭabʿ), which is essentially the same. Nasafi explains that the core of both doctrines is the impossibility of the effect not occurring when its cause exists — this is similar to the naturalists (materialists) who attribute effects to the nature of the substrate, but these two attribute them to God, yet under obligation. Nasafi then refutes this: the idea of obligation on God implies that God is compelled, and that a human who performs a cause in some substrate obliges God to create the effect, leaving God no power to refrain. This, Nasafi asserts, is clearly corrupt and obviously flawed.

Key Terms
إيجاب الخلقة
إيجاب الطبع
أهل الطبائع
اضطرار
قدرة الامتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Nazzam and al-Qalānisi held different views on generated acts.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly states that there is no difference between them; both base their view on obligation upon God, making them essentially the same.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the consequence of the doctrine of obligation (ījāb) upon God?
A) It affirms God's wisdom and power.
B) It implies that God is compelled and has no power to refrain.
C) It denies causation altogether.
D) It affirms human free will.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of obligation upon God is corrupt because it implies divine compulsion, contradicting God's absolute power and freedom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever expected God to act in a certain way because of something you did or because of how He created you? How does al-Nasafi's refutation challenge that expectation and call you to trust His freedom rather than demand from Him?
?
When you pray or do good deeds, do you sometimes feel that God 'owes' you a positive outcome? How does the doctrine of obligation being 'clearly corrupt' expose the hidden entitlement in your heart, and what would it look like to surrender that false claim?
?
If you truly believed that God has absolute power to refrain from acting (qudrat al-imitināʿ), how would that change the way you respond when your plans fail or your prayers seem unanswered? Are you willing to worship a God who is free, even when His freedom contradicts your expectations?

The Maturidi View and Thumama's Opinion on Generated Acts
Nasafi presents the Maturidi custom-based causation and Thumama's no-agent view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position on causation as divine custom (ʿāda) and contrast it with Thumama ibn al-Ashras's view that generated acts have no agent.

Core Idea
God’s custom, not necessity, links causes to their effects.

Nasafi presents the Maturidi view that generated effects are not necessitated by their causes; rather, God freely chooses to create them as His custom. He then cites the more radical opinion of Thumama ibn al-Ashras, who denies any agent for such acts. This lesson clarifies both positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the literal translation. Notice the two distinct views presented.
The passage has two parts: 'And according to us...' and 'And Thumama claimed...'.
2
Identify the Maturidi position
Focus on the first part: substrate devoid of phenomena, God not obliged, God acts by choice, but has made it a custom. Understand that causation is regular custom, not logical necessity.
Key terms: العادة (custom), العرف (customary), اختيار (choice).
3
Identify Thumama's position
Read the second part: Thumama claims that generated acts have no agent at all. Contrast this with the Maturidi view that God is the agent (by custom).
Thumama's view is more extreme: even God is not the agent of generated acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندنا أن خلو المحل عن هذه المعاني عند وجود ما يعد من العرف سببًا لها جائز، والله تعالى لا يجب عليه أن يفعل شيئًا منها في المحل وما يفعل يفعل باختياره، غير أنه أجرى العادة بأن يفعل ذلك كله. وزعم ثمامة بن الأشرس أن ما سمي عندهم متولدًا كل ذلك أفعال لا فاعل لها.
And according to us, the substrate being devoid of these phenomena while that which is considered by custom (al-ʿurf) a cause for them exists is possible. And God the Exalted is not obliged to do any of them in the substrate, and what He does, He does by His choice. But He has made it a custom (al-ʿāda) to do all that. And Thumāma ibn al-Ashras claimed that what they call generated — all that are actions with no agent.
Source pages p. 945

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi view followed by Thumama ibn al-Ashras's opinion. Nasafi states the Maturidi position: it is possible for the substrate (the thing that receives an effect) to be devoid of those generated phenomena even when what is customarily considered a cause exists. God is not obliged to create any such effect; He acts solely by His own choice. However, God has made it His custom (al-ʿāda) to create these effects in conjunction with their customary causes. This is the Maturidi explanation: causation is regular custom, not logical necessity. Then Nasafi reports Thumama ibn al-Ashras's view, which is even more radical: what are called generated acts are actions without any agent at all. The text ends here; Nasafi's refutation of Thumama is not included in this window.

Key Terms
العادة
العرف
اختيار
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Maturidi view denies causation entirely.
Correction: The Maturidi view affirms regular custom (ʿāda), meaning God consistently creates effects after their customary causes, but denies necessary connection between cause and effect.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's Maturidi view, why do generated effects occur after their customary causes?
A) Because God is obliged to create them.
B) Because the nature of the substrate necessitates them.
C) Because God has made it His custom (ʿāda) to create them after those causes.
D) Because humans create them directly.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school explains causation as God's established custom, preserving divine transcendence, while Thumama denied any agent for generated acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fire burn something, do you instinctively attribute the burning to the fire's nature, or do you pause to recognise that God is the true agent who could withold that effect? In what area of your life do you rely most on secondary causes, forgetting that God is not obliged?
?
Thumama claimed that generated acts have no agent. How would your daily routine change if you truly believed that no human effort or natural process has any inherent effect, and that every outcome is directly from God? Is your reliance on means a form of hidden idolatry?

The Mu'tazilite Account of Causal Responsibility
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite argument that the agent of a cause is morally responsible for its effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu‘tazilites argue that the agent of a cause is morally responsible for its effects based on the effects occurring in accordance with the agent’s will and intention.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites hold that effects follow the agent's will, making him blameworthy.

In this passage, al-Nasafi presents a core Mu‘tazilite argument regarding causality and moral responsibility. They contend that just as a person’s own actions are governed by his will, the effects he causes — like a push causing movement — are also produced according to his intention. This, they assert, makes him culpable for those effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the distinction between reality (ḥaqīqa) and judgment (ḥukm).
The passage is divided into two parts: a description of causality and a moral conclusion.
2
Understand the analogy
The Mu‘tazilites compare causing effects to intentionally pushing a body. Identify how the analogy supports their claim that effects follow the agent’s will.
The push example is central to their argument.
3
Reflect on the moral implication
Ask yourself: why does the author say that if the effect were not the agent’s act, blame would be impossible? Connect this to the idea of moral responsibility.
This is the key point of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما جمهور المعتزلة فإنهم يزعمون أن تلك المعاني في محالها توجد على وفق إرادة فاعل السبب وقصده كما توجد أفعاله القائمة به على وفق إرادته وقصده فإنه إذا أراد أن يتحرك الجسم حركة يسيرة دفعه دفعة خفيفة، ومتى أراد أن يتحرك حركة قوية دفعه دفعة قوية، وكذلك الحال في الألم وذهاب السهم وأشباه ذلك، هذا من حيث الحقيقة، ومن حيث الحكم أن فاعل أسبابها يلام عليها ويعاقب ويعذب عليه ويعاقب فإن الإنسان يعاتب ويعاقب على جرح الغير وإيلامه، ولو لم يكن ذلك فعله لما توجهت عليه اللائمة ولا استحق عليه العقوبة المتلفة أو الناهكة إذ استيجاب ذلك واستحقاقه على فعل الغير محال.
As for the majority of the Mu'tazilites, they claim that those meanings (effects) in their loci exist in accordance with the will and intention of the agent of the cause, just as his acts subsisting in him exist in accordance with his will and intention. For if he wants the body to move with a slight motion, he pushes it with a light push; and when he wants it to move with a strong motion, he pushes it with a strong push. And similarly is the matter in pain, the flight of an arrow, and the like. This is in terms of reality; in terms of judgment, the agent of their causes is blamed for them, punished, and tormented. So a person is reproached and punished for wounding another and causing him pain. And if that were not his act, reproach would not be directed at him, nor would he deserve the penalty that destroys or harms, since deserving that on the act of another is impossible.
Source pages p. 946

Commentary
This passage describes the Mu'tazilite position on causation and moral responsibility. According to them, the effects (like pain, movement of an arrow) are produced in their loci (e.g., the body) according to the will and intention of the agent who performs the cause. They draw an analogy: if a person wants a body to move slightly, he gives a light push; if he wants a strong movement, he gives a strong push. Similarly, pain, arrow flight, etc., follow from the agent's intention. Consequently, in terms of legal and moral judgment, the agent is blameworthy and punishable for these effects. For example, one is held accountable for wounding another and causing pain. If the effect were not his act, it would be unjust to blame him. The Mu'tazilites argue that holding someone responsible for the act of another is impossible.

The Mu'tazilites argue that since the agent's will determines the effect, he deserves blame and punishment for it.

Do you actually trust God's justice to account for your true intentions, or do you fear His judgment more for the external results?

Causality and Created Things
